Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

Mr SPEAKER CRt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 10.30 a.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Proposed Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline
services in Australia; (ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
by Mr Jacobi, Mr Charles Jones, Mr Les McMahon, Mr Morris and Mr Mountford.
Petitions received.
Excise on Beer
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. The hotel industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places where the people may partake of the three basic needs of life: a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The hotel industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The hotel industry is almost entirely within the private sector and makes a large contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.
4. The hotel industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
5. The hotel industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry's 5811 hotels in Australia.
7. The very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded the growth of the Australian hotel industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.

9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the hotel industry to pass on such increases to the consumer by way ofhigher prices for beer.
i 1. Higher excise would be only the starting point for an
upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The hotel industry of Australia already is gravely handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth is at a standstill; and, inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and place of the Australian Hotel industry in the social and economic life of the nation.
2. Accept that the viability of the hotel industry is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the hotel needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les McMahon, Mr Millar and Mr Scott.
Petitions received.
Taxation: Child Care Expenses
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
A substantial contribution to Australia's economy by the payment of taxation and to the quality oflife of fellow Australians, is made by parents in the work-force who have preschool children_
Taxation relief for adequate child-care is necessary to enable these patents to avail themselves of child-care facilities in order to take paid employment.
Your petitioners therefore humhly pray that tax-payers who incur child-care expenses in order to earn income should be able to have those expenses exempt from income taxation in the same way as other tax-payers can deduct business expenses from their assessable income.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Beazley.
Petition received.

That the level of Benefits for all unemployed people, and including people receiving sickness benefits and pensions, be raised to at least 120 per cent of the poverty line.

Petition received.
Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(1) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.
Petition received.
Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned Australian citizens showeth:
That the last increase in the Supplementary Assistance or rent allowance payable to Invalid and Age pensioners was made in 1974;
That since that time, average weekly rents, as gauged by increases in average Queensland Housing Commission rents, have risen by approximately 150 per cent and continue to rise at an alarming rate.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will at the earliest Oppojltmity index Supplementary Assistance to increases in theeost of living by the same biannual process applied to increases in the pension itself.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Rosemount Repatriation Hospital
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of undersigned Australian citizens showeth:
Rosemount Repatriation Hospital has been of inestimable value to eligible repatriation beneficiaries for many years, and the closure or dispersal of the facilities available at the hospital would be a retrograde step.
The proposed decision to close Rosemount Repatriation Hospital through active discouragement by departmental administrative officers of any further referrals by medical officers of patients for medically prescribed occupational therapy is objectionable.
Closure of Rosemount Repatriatio~ Hospital would
severely disadvantage veterans in Brisbane's northern suburbs, as well as their relatives and friends who would be required to travel a great distance in order to visit hospitalised veterans at either of the two alternative Repatriation hospitals.
Closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital and transfer of patients to Greenslopes would greatly add to the Administrative burden of that repatriation and general hospital.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will reverse its announced decision to close the Rosemount Repatriation Hospital.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.

Service Pensions

That continued use of animal ingredients in cosmetic products, and the inhumane use of animals in scientific research for cosmetic products is abhorrent and barbaric.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and the Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned respectfully showeth. Noting that:
(l) Though the abolition of fees for post secondary education has not of its own accord improved access to education, the re-imposition of fees will make it more difficult for students from average families as well as many mature age students to commence or continue tertiary education.
(2) While supporting a government funded scheme of short term emergency loans, we believe the introduction of a comprehensive loans scheme would undermine the TEAS scheme and put students and graduates into debt to finance companies and discourage poorer students from attending post secondary institutions call in capitals.
We the undersigned, oppose any attempts to introduce fees or a comprehensive loans scheme for post secondary education and call upon the government to restate its commitment to education without tuitors fees or loans and assure students that no such options are under consideration for the 1981 budget.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hurford.
Petition received.
Defence Service Home Loans
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the members of the Women's Services of the 1939-45 War not at present considered eligible for Defence Service Home Loans should qualify as "Australian Soldiers" under the Defence Service Homes Act. They volunteered for service with the Naval, Military and Air Forces, serving wherever they were sent in whatever capacity they were required and yet are refused Defence Service Home Loans.
They took the oath to resist His Majesty's enemies which meant to fight His Majesty's enemies, and although not used in actual combat in war zones, they were available for that use and had a sufficiently grave need arisen would have been used, to serve with distinction as have women of other lands.
In accordance with the Acts and Regulations of the period, the women of the Military Forces had enlisted for service anywhere, some of the Naval women to sail in any waters, others with service on warships optional, while the women of the Air Force could have been sent to Papua New Guinea or other territories under the control or care of Australia.
Ex-servicemen of the Royal Australian Navy, the Australian Imperial Force and the Royal Australian Air Force who volunteered in the 1939-45 War but were not sent to War Zones are granted home loans. Not to grant eligibility to all Ex-servicewomen of 1938-45 War is unjust and unfair.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that you favourably consider amending the Defence Service Homes Act by repealing Sub-section 4 (2A) (b) (c) (d) of the Principal Act as amended by No. 137 of 1978, and grant eligibility to all membe~s of the Women's Royal Australian Naval Service, the Australian Women's Army Service, the Women's Auxiliary Australian Air Force, the Australian Army Medical Women's Service, and the Voluntary Aid Detachment of 1939-45 War.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Jarman.
Petition received.

Australian Broadcasting Commission: Melbourne Showband
To the Honounible, the Speaker and Members of the Australian Parliament in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Victoria respectfully showeth that:
As regular viewers and listeners of the ABC Melbourne Showband we were disappointed to learn of the dismissal of seventeen members of this outstanding orchestra.
We have had a great deal of pleasure over the past years viewing and listening to this band's fine music. It is the only full-time light orchestra in Australia and we are disgusted to think that such talented musicians should be dismissed in this manner.
We therefore call on the Federal Government to rescind this decision and make more money available for the continuance of the full Showband.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Cunningham.
Petition received.
FRASER GOVERNMENT
Notice of Motion
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-l give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House condemns the Government for-
(1) its erratic and unstable behaviour, its preoccupation with its internal dissension and the paralysis of the proper functions of government and of this Parliament because of the Government's total distraction with these problems;
(2) the unfair and inequitable manner proposed for the elimination of or reduction in important community programs;
(3) the serious damage caused to the federal system by savage and unfair reductions in funding provided for the States;

That this House condemns the Government for-
(1) its erratic and unstable behaviour, its preoccupation with its internal dissension and the paralysis of the proper functions of government and of this Parliament because of the Government's total distraction with these problems;
(2) the unfair and inequitable manner proposed for the elimination of or reduction in important community programs;
(3) the serious damage caused to the federal system by savage and unfair reductions in funding provided for the States;
(4) the deceitful way in which the States were deprived of $70m to which the Australian people were entitled; and
(5) the extraordinary behaviour of the Prime Minister and the Treasurer in seeking to publicly humiliate the Premiers through shoddy leaks to the media.
The Opposition does not take this action lightly. Formal moves to censure the government of the day are made only when the state of the nation's affairs demands them-when the government has patently failed to fulfil the trust given to it by the people. This describes precisely the condition of the Government today.
Over the past 10 days, and especially over the past 48 hours, various events have given the nation an extraordinary insight into the degraded state into which this Government has descended: The disarray, the turmoil, the confusion and the incompetence of the Government itself, and the consequent paralysis and corruption of the Parliament. Because of these events, the truth is now apparent. Behind that stony Easter Island image, nothing stirs but personal and malevolent ambition.
The forces that motivate this Government have nothing to do with the welfare of the nation; they have all to do with the survival in power of the Government. The new health program, the Lynch

2096 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Fraser Government

Committee proposals, and the results of the Premiers Conference, show that what this Government intends for the next decade is not the great leap forward that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcom Fraser) promised during the election last October, but a timid scramble backwards. They show that all that this Government has in mind for the 1980s is nostalgia for the 19508, when the hiwes were urged on to ever greater affluence and the have-nots were left to fend for themselves.
The nation is entitled to ask why the Government is in such a dishevelled state. The answer can be found in the Prime Minister's contemptuous treatment of the Premiers and the experience of his former Minister for Industrial Relations. The petty vindictiveness of the Prime Minister towards the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock) and his selective leaking of confidential Cabinet decisions in order to score cheap points off the Premiers, show that the Prime Minister will go to extreme lengths to protect his ascendancy over his colleagues.
The honourable member for Kooyong has now told us the reasons for this degradation of parliamentary and government affairs: The Prime Minister's manic determination to get his way, his suspicion of his colleagues, the subversion of government in factional interests, the bitter tensions that exist within the coalition. The honourable member for Kooyong has put it on the scoreboard. These are the reasons that, seven months after the election, the Government has achieved nothing, save a few favours for a few friends. These are the reasons that the nation's affairs are in such a disorganised, ineffective state. These are the reasons that the Prime Minister and his colleagues deserve the most severe censure of this Parliament and the nation.
The Prime Minister has nobody but himself to blame for the latest crisis in which he finds himself embroiled. He was not content with simply taking the States to the cleaners to the tune of $70m. He was determined to ensure that everyone should know how clever he was, and how tough he could be in a confrontation with the Premiers. He embarked on a strategy on Monday in which he set up the Treasurer (Mr Howard) to leak the details of their cleverness to selected journalists. When this did not produce the desired headlines, the necessary stage-managed publicity, the Prime Minister called a background briefing of journalists from the Herald and Weekly Times group of newspapers, among them Mr Trevor Hawkins from the Sun News-Pictorial, Mr Wallace Brown of the Courier Mail, and Mr Geof Kitney of the Perth Daily News. This briefing in the Prime Minister's office took place on Tuesday night, and

produced the news stories yesterday morning which so incensed the Western Australian Premier, Sir Charles Court.
It was from these incidents that flowed the extraordinary sequence of events last night in which the Prime Minister and the Treasurer between them held four Press conferences to try to extricate the Prime Minister from the consequences of his smart-Alick behaviour. I emphasise that these were, of course, Press conferences. At no stage did the Prime Minister seek to come into this House and attempt to justify his deceit of the Premiers, or to explain why he thought it necessary to leak to the Press information that could only be seen as humiliating to all State governments and contemptuous of the Australian people. Nor has there yet been any explanation to the Parliament why this Prime Minister believes he has some special exemption from the normal Westminster conventions that preclude Ministers from breaching Cabinet confidentiality.
As past events have demonstrated, however, this Prime Minister obviously believes the usual rules and proper standards concerning a great range of attitudes, among them trust, loyalty and honesty, both in personal relationships and in his conduct of government, simply do not apply to him. The honourable member for Kooyong made this perfectly clear in his speech to the House last week explaining his resignation from the Fraser Ministry. What has happened this week serves only to substantiate the very strong criticisms of the Prime Minister that were contained in that speech. Let me remind the House that, like the Peacock resignation, the events of the past 48 hours sprang from that much abused political tactic, the background briefing. In the case of the honourable member for Kooyong, so-called senior government officials were the source of the story that appeared in the Australian on 14 April concerning allegations damaging to his political and personal integrity. As the honourable member said last week, it was this story, and the Prime Minister's gross disloyalty to him in not denying it in detail, that finally provoked his resignation.
The source of the Australian story has not been disclosed. Yet ironically we now have the same tactic of the background briefing being used, again in an issue concerning the Prime Minister, again with the same euphemistic source of senior government officials, and again causing political fall-out for the Government. The difference this time is that the Prime Minister has been caught out. He has been forced to admit his direct involvement, both in authorising the Treasurer to leak to the Press, and then by calling his own background briefing to reinforce his disclosure of

Fraser Government

First, there is the broad question of Commonwealth-State relations. Clearly the Prime Minister and the Treasurer believe that conferences with the Premiers are, as one Premier now describes them, no more than exercises in bluff in which the biggest cheat wins. There is no doubt the principle was upheld on this occasion. Equally clearly, this Government is utterly contemptuous of such conferences as forums for meaningful and responsible negotiations. Second, there is the matter of economic management.

The Prime Minister has prevaricated, he has evaded, he has sneered, he has threatened, he has boasted and he has used his staff to hector in an attempt to intimidate the Australian Broadcasting Commission into scrapping video film of a stumbling and shifty interview he gave last night. After that appalling performance on being confronted by questions he did not want to answer on events his own foolish actions had precipitated, he tried to censor the news. His Press secretary, Mr Barnett, who gracelessly is accommodated in the officials enclosure opposite, thundered around the Press Gallery corridors last night like a half-pay reject from Cop Shop trying to bully and badger the ABC journalists into censoring the news, and when that did not work, levelling threats against them. That is, this is not just a repudiation of the Premiers but it is a rejection of the rights of the people of this country. Having succeeded by shaving $70m off the funds that Cabinet had agreed the Commonwealth could afford, the Prime Minister could not resist boasting of his success or, when his cheap. sneering backfired, pushing the Treasurer forward to take his place and to take the blame. This is not only an arrogant and irresponsible means of economic management, it is also wholly consistent with this Government's erratic and ad hoc administration of the last 5f years.
Finally, there are the ethics of the Prime Minister's behaviour. We on this side of the House have long held the view that this Government cannot

What that demonstrates, of course, is that Parliament continues to be as much of an irrelevance to this Prime Minister as it has always been to his Government. Question Time is a farce; the Standing Orders are misused as an obstruction to proper scrutiny and accountability; the Notice Paper is no more than a stock-list of deferred debates and questions on notice, some of which have been waiting for six months for an answer. 2098 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Fraser Government

of information legislation which frees no information at all is jammed in the Senate, and we are all still waiting for the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) to bring before the Parliament his foreshadowed amendments to the Broadcasting and Television Act, a Bill which has all the earmarks of another sell-out of the public interest to this Government's great and powerful friends.
What we can make of this is that Parliament has done nothing because this Government has nothing for it to do. It reacts; it does not initiate. It has no perceived objective except to stay in office by gimmickry and deceit, and no vision beyond the political problems of the immediate 24 hours. All these aspects of this Government's conduct are crystallised in the events of the last 48 hours.
No single issue better characterises this Government's lack of integrity, or better illustrates its fundamental dishonesty, than does the issue of health care. Of all the broken promises, the dishonoured commitments, the smart, mealymouthed slogans, the double dealing and the double standards, and the deliberate and calculated lies of the last 5t years it is the Fraser Government's policies and actions on health care which epitomise its basic character, its subservience to wealth and privilege and its mean and grubby behaviour towards the rest of the Australian people.
We on this side of the House see the question of health care quite differently from our opponents. We believe health care is a right, not a privilege. That is why we introduced the Medibank scheme in 1974. It is why we remain committed to the reintroduction of a universal health insurance scheme at the first opportunity after the next election. If the new health program is an example of the Government's duplicity, the Lynch committee's proposals-coupled with the results of the Premiers Conference-demonstrate equally well the Government's timidity. Three and a half years ago the Prime Minister announced that we were ready, as he put it, 'to stride into a new era of prosperity and development'. Over the past fortnight we have been given a glimpse of this Government's vision of what this era contains. What a miserable, dispirited vision it is!
In the Lynch committee program, and throughout the results of the Premiers Conference, the Government is demonstrating that it has decided to run away from its responsibilities. It somehow sees virtues in this-virtue that is given the name of small government. In the real world, of course, small government is meaningless. In the real

world, pressures and expectations exist that demand government thatcis aware, government that is realistic, government -in contact with the people and not, as the Lynch committee would have it, government cut off from them. In this real world, there are more than two million people trying to survive in poverty. For with all its potential, this society somehow manages to have one of the most inequitable distributions of wealth in the industrialised world. Social and economic injustices abound in welfare programs, in housing, in health, education, in the law, in the distribution of wealth, income and opportunity. Discrimination plagues such minority groups as migrants and Aboriginal Australians, who suffer under one of the highest incidences of disease in the world. The drive for equal rights for women has all but been abandoned. The need for rational urban planning, housing and regional development programs has been denied.
Individual citizens--no match on their own for ever-growing corporations and their everwidening power-deserve more influence in their jobs and in the market place and greater protection for their environment. This is the reality and the Government has run away from it. The only vision that stirs its leader is that of power and how to maintain it, no matter how malevolent, damaging and divisive the measures that have to be adopted to achieve that end. Yesterday's events show that he will abuse his relationship with the Press, ignore the Parliament and even subvert national economic policy-making to maintain his ascendancy over those he imagines to be a threat to him and his position.
Everywhere in the nation's administration is confusion, contradiction and incompetence. The Fraser Government and its ways have become a farce. Long after the people have swept it out of office the joke will be going round the pubs that the Fraser Government was the government that decided to sell a cement works in the city where it had just closed down all the building industry. Any normal Prime Minister who presided over such chaos would resign, even if only out of selfrespect. But this Prime Minister is not normal. He gained power with whatever unsavory weapons were to hand. He is obviously determined to hang on to it, no matter what the cost to the nation or what damage it does to his own party.
By this motion the House seeks to recall the Prime Minister and his colleagues to their senses. It seeks to remind them that Australians expect more from their Government than they have received from this Administration-confusion and incompetence conce~ning everything but the

Let us look at what else the Government has done to the taxpayer~ of Australia. Let us look at the health program. The Government has dec, manded that when a person goes to hospital as an outpatient he or she must pay $15 a time. Can one imagine the situation of a person who is dependent upon a dialysis machine and who has to go to hospital at least four times a month and the situation of those suffering from skin diseases who have to attend the outpatients' department perhaps once a week? The Government has demanded that the States extract extra money from these poor, unfortunate taxpayers who suffer ill health. What sort of a health scheme is it that is run only on a Treasury basis? How can a government run a health scheme on the basis of monetary allocation and not on the needs of the sick, the poor and the underprivileged? The Government has demanded that the States extract millions of dollars from the Australian people in their efforts to keep their health. What sort of a nation is it that cannot afford a health program?
The Government guaranteed in its previous election campaign that it would maintain Medibank. What a joke that is. That is one example of the many promises the Government made. The Government promised that it would maintain wage indexation and tax indexation. It intended to maintain all the rights and privileges of the Australian nation. The Prime Minister has broken promise after promise. The chickens have come home to roost now because the Government's own kith and kin, the wool growers and others, are sending urgent telegrams saying: 'Look what you have done in relation to wool testing. It will affect 100,000 wool growers and the test will not even be accepted internationally'.
Even the Prime Minister's own supporters cannot tolerate him any more. When the Premiers as a group say that he is not to be trusted, why should he remain in office? If the Prime Minister were to go to the nation now on the basis of this so-called arrangement with the States, 'every Premier, irrespective of his politics, would vote against him. They would oppose him and ask for him to be removed from office because he is of no value to the nation. The reason is this: Unless a person has integrity and political honesty he or she is not entitled to hold office. Thecbehaviour of the Prime Minister. right across the spectrum of this nation, whether it be in relation to national affairs or international affairs, is of deceit and deception. It does not matter what the Prime Minister tells us in this Parliament, he is prone to deceive us. He is prone to have arrangements made

with the United States of America that do not mean what they say an<b,~rrangements made with the States that do not ffie;;an what they say. He has given indications in thIS Parliament of his intentions but he never carries them out. Promises made at election time are never carried out. These are the problems in relation to what is said--
Mr Sainsbury-Oh, phooey.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-'Oh, phooey" says the interjector at the back of the chamber. He holds a marginal seat that we will take next time there is an election. Let us look at the record of the Government. Let us take one issue that affects the States. I refer to the area of pre-schools. I was the Minister who went around to every Premier and who at the time had responsibilty for looking after children under the age of five years. I said to them: 'I will put it in writing that we promise to provide 75 per cent of the recurrent costs oflooking after pre-school children'. This we did. If we look at New South Wales, for example-this situation applies right across the board-we find now that the Commonwealth's contribution to recurrent costs for pre-school children is a mere 30 per cent. If we look at itfrom the point of view of children under five being cared for in New South Wales, that State now has to pay $8.2m for this care. The figure in 1976-77 was a mere $1.7m. Is this not symptomatic of all the things this Government has done? If people go to Queanbeyan Hospital they will pay more; if they send children to the pre-school at Queanbeyan they will pay more.
Let us have a look at what has happened from the point of view of housing grants. In 1975 $127m was allocated for housing grants. In 1980-81, $90m was allocated. This represents a reduction in monetary terms and a 50 per cent reduction in real terms. Let us look at dwelling construction. In 1977 New South Wales was able to construct 3,000 houses for low income earners. But with the money which has been allocated by this Government it will be able to construct 1,200 houses. Is it any wonder that the States are asking: 'What are you going to do for us?' Australian taxpayers are involved. Tturn now to the question of looking after children in institutions. Again the Government says that this is another State responsibility. In 1976 the Government gave a measly $5 for each child in an institution. That allocation has not been increased since 1976. So the States-I use New South Wales as an examplenow have to provide $27.50 for each child. This Government gives nothing. That applies right across the board. The States have now been given a signal thatthis Government will not provide any unemployment benefit to 16 and 17 year oIds. The

Fraser Government

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2101

States will have to pay for their institutional or other care. This Government gives them nothing. And this is a nationalgovernment interested in welfare!
Let us look now at the meals on wheels situation. In the three years that we were in government we increased the subsidy for meals on wheels on three occasions. This Government has increased it once. That service is not able to keep going as it cannot: keep up with the costs. The States are having to provide meals for the pensioners and the poor as this Government is not prepared to do it. The States have to do this using taxpayers' money in the States. The meals on wheels operation requires vehicles to operate and this Government has not increased the subsidy for these vehicles but has escalated petrol tax by 400 per cent. In social welfare programs-whether we are talking about pre-schools, housing, juvenile problems or the care of young or old people-this Government has failed at every turn. The States could use $280m. They would use it for Australians who are paying taxes now. What right has this Government as trustee to say that it will appropriate that money to itself because it has a mandate so to do? Where is this Government's mandate to do any of these things? Where is its mandate to deny care to children? Where is its mandate to whittle down the States' housing finances? Where is its mandate to deny help to the underprivileged, whether they be in institutions or anything of that nature?
Mr Ruddock-Your mandate would increase taxes.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-That is just it. This Government's mandate is: Do not increase the taxes federally but sock the people really hard through the States. The honourable member for Dundas should try to tell the people in his electorate that their taxes have been reduced. They will say to him: 'Ruddock, you are out of your mind. We are paying a fortune for the care of our children. We cannot get housing loans at reasonable interest rates. We are paying enormous costs which the States have to impose for transport, health care, and every other charge'. What a thimble and pea trick it is for this Government to say: 'We will reduce the taxes to you but you will pay through the nose'. The whole idea of federalism was to give everybody in Australia a fair share of the wealth. This Government has approached it on the basis that the States should try to do the best they can. The Government knows better than anyone else that some States have more capacity to levy taxes than others. Is this Government running federalism on the basis that there has to be double taxation? Obviously it is.

There has to be a Federal tax. There has to be a State tax. That is the Government's policy. If we go to any of the people in Australia today they say 'What do we need a national government for? It opted out of everything'-except selling this country to overseas interests. The Government has opted out of everything. What is it going to do now as a national government? Why does it not talk about being involved in education? Why does it not talk about being involved in health? Why is it not involved in housing? Why is it not involved in urban transport where the Government has slashed programs to ribbons? The Government promised in every election campaign, for example, to assist the country lines program. After the election the Government deprived the program of the moneys that were promised. Everywhere that the Government has made promises it has failed to fulfil them.
What does it mean? The interjection referred to taxes. The Government says: 'Perhaps we will drop Federal income tax rates by $2 or $3 but the mugs outside won't know that. They will have to pay another $20 or $30 through State taxes but we will go away as being good economic managers'. I make the point-luckily this is the point-that the State Ministers for Health will be here tomorrow. I hope that those Ministers tear the Government to ribbons on the basis of how the Government misled them. The Premiers are coming back. They would love to come back tomorrow. They ought to be here tomorrow to tear the Government limb from limb on the basis of how the Government deceived them by saying that it could not afford any more money.
The Prime Minister went out and told the Press that it was beyond any possibility that the States could get another one per cent increase. In fact, as Sir Charles Court has said, under the arrangements, the promises made to him in February 1976, he was entitled to another 4 per cent from the point of view of keeping up with the costs of living. Inflation is spiralling. Who has to pay the costs of inflation? Interest rates now are 17 per cent or more. Who is paying that? The Australian taxpayer. Where is the Government's economic management at any level we want to look at? How dreadful is it to think that we had a parliament in session yesterday and not one of the Government's leaders could find time to come into this House to discuss the problems as to why the States were upset, as to why the Premiers of every State were concerned as to what was to happen to their programs! The Prime Minister could not have cared less. It was not until Sir Charles Court said: 'Listen, unless you go in and make a public confession and show how weak you are, I

That is where the influence came from. Top marks to Sir Charles Court! Even though his politics are poor, his principles in this area are accurate.

Mr MALCOLM FRASER (Wannon-Prime Minister) (11.12)-This Government in 1975 and in successive elections and with record majorities was given a mandate to govern this country well. We wi 11 discharge that duty through this Parliament and through future parliaments:
Opposition members interjecting Mr SPEAKER-I ask honourable members on my left not to keep up the interjection level which means that the right honourable gentleman cannot be heard. I ask all honourable members to observe that. Mr MALCOLM FRASER-In 1975 this Government was first given a mandate to govern this country well. After that election and through successive elections and with record majorities this duty has been discharged, and it will continue to be through this Parliament and through successive parliaments. The Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) spoke of the state of the nation as though there was no hope and confidence in the wider community. How far apart can he be from the feelings, the sense of confidence and optimism, that is now shared in every corner of Australia? How far is he from the imaginative decisions that we have taken over the last few days in health, in the Review of Commonwealth Functions and, indeed, in the Premiers Conference itself? The Leader of the Opposition and the Australian Labor Party can only look to the past and the prescriptions of the past which show that they believe it is government, government decisions, government bureaucracies and large spending which must determine it all. A classic example is health where the Labor Party believes the Government through taxpayers' funds should provide everything, should spend everything and do everything. We happen to have a different belief. We believe that available funds should be concentrated on the disadvantaged and on those in need. We do not believe that taxpayers' money should be used to pay unnecessary subsidies to those who are better off in the community. We do not believe in undermining individual responsibility by centralised bureaucracy. We believe the user should pay and the user is entitled to the

influence that comes from paying. We do not believe in dual control ofc,.the Federal and State governments over Australia's hospitals. We believe the States have responsibilities that they ought to discharge. Labor's arrogant assumption that Canberra knows best is false indeed.
The central part of this debate is whether the people of Australia, the taxpayers, should have dollars to spend on their own account or whether the Government should continue to absorb larger and larger funds, leaving less and less freedom to people throughout this country. A very essential principle consistent with political freedom is economic freedom, freedom to make decisions and to choose and determine one's own future. Labor's high taxes, big bureaucracy and interfering regulations undermined this economy in the past and they would do it again. We well know that the predictions of the Leader of the Opposition about the Australian economy time and again have proved to be so very wrong.

Let us come to the canards which are told by this Prime Minister and this Government in terms of what the philosophy is which justifies their position. In terms of their concepts, they say they are not a government of big government; they are the government of lesser government. Let us look at the facts as distinct from what they put out. Between 1972-73 and 1975-76 Commonwealth Budget receipts as a proportion of gross domestic product averaged 23.9 per cent. This was during the period of the Labor Government. The percentage has grown inexorably under this Government. Between 1976-77 and 1980-81 the amount taken by the Commonwealth Government as a proportion of gross domestic product has gone up from an average of 23.9 per cent during the period of the Labor Government to 25.8 per cent.
This Government is not a government of lesser government; it is a government of less equitable government. What is the evidence of this? The evidence is clear. One should look at the Government's approach in terms of the sort of regulatory mechanism it is going to impose on this community. The Government says it is wrong to have a mechanism imposed upon industry in terms of the Prices Justification Tribunal or in terms of the Trade Practices Commission so it either abolishes these mechanisms or emasculates them. But at the same time the Government heaps upon heap the mechanisms which it imposes in the industrial relations field to the point where the Industrial Relations Bureau, this monstrosity, is maintainedagainst the advice of the employers,. against the advice of the trade union movement, against the advice of the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission and against the advice of his own Minister who has just resigned because he could not stand the stench of this Government. This is the living proof that this is not a government committed to smaller or lesser government. As I said before, it is a goverrtment which can be properly described only as a government of the privileged, by the privileged and for the privileged.

Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (11.42)-1 would like in the first part of my remarks to deal with what I believe to be the central issue in this debate and, most particularly, the central issue of the events of the past 48 hours. That is the proposition that in some way the Commonwealth Government has robbed the States of approximately $70m. It is a charge that 1 can understand. I can understand why it has been made in the circumstances of the past 48 hours but during this debate, because I regard that issue as being absolutely crucial to the credibility of what has been said by the Opposition and to the merits of this debate, I want to examine the approach of the Commonwealth to the Premiers Conference in its proper perspective.
All of us know-none better than people who sit on the Opposition front benches-that in any discussion or in negotiation the people negotiating on behalf of the parties have a certain capacity and a certain flexibility. They are not sent to the negotiating table with one, and one only, instruction. They are given guidance. They are told what is the ambit of their authority and are given guidance as to what they should do. The truth is that the Federal Cabinet gave to the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and me, as the Cabinet has done on the occasion of earlier Premiers Conferences, authority to make a decision on the day of the Conference as to the final amount that should be given. That fact cannot be stressed too much. We were not told, when the Cabinet took its decision, that we had to give the States the same in real terms plus absorption. We were told that

under no circumstances should we go beyond that figure, that we should make an assessment on the day in the light of economic circumstances and budgetary considerations as to the appropriate amount. For it to be suggested in those circumstances that we have in some way robbed the States and that we have in some way deceived them is totally to misrepresent and to misunderstand the nature of a conference such as this. It is a total misunderstanding of the nature of Cabinet giving to Ministers a general authority and a total misunderstanding of what happened on the day of that Conference.
As I have already indicated, at that Conference negotiations started on the basis of there being an offer by the Commonwealth of 8 per cent plus absorption. After very lengthy discussion that figure was increased by an amount of approximately $27m as a result of a decision on the part of the Commonwealth-a decision which was taken by the Prime Minister and me in consultation with the other five Ministers who were present at the Conference. That decision was to increase the amount by $27m by providing to the States of South Australia, Tasmania and Victoria a sufficient additional supplement so that their total increase would not be less than 8 per cent, thus increasing the overall amount to about 8+ per cent.
After dinner and after lengthy discussion between the seven Commonwealth Ministers, it was decided to increase the amount. I repeat that that decision was taken only after that lengthy discussion. It was not a unanimous discussion. As everyone would expect, dilTering points of view were expressed in this discussion. It was decided to increase that amount to 9 pel' cent plus absorption. I regard that as an entirely proper, predictable and justifiable procedure for the Commonwealth to take at a conference such as this. To suggest that this is a procedure that in some way robs the States, deceives the States and is in some way dishonourable on the part of the Commonwealth is to misunderstand the nature of these types of negotiations.
We indicated very clearly throughout the entirety of. the Conference just precisely what our position was. Therefore, 1 want to emphasise as powerfully as I can that the proposition that in some way the States have been robbed of $70m is a proposition without substance. We are not, as the Prime Minister and others have said during this debate, talking about whether it is the States' money or the Commonwealth's money. We are talking about it being the money of the taxpayers of Australia. We were given authority by our

PERSONAL EXPLANATION Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications)-Mr Speaker, I wish to make a personal explanation.
Mr SPEAKER-The right honourable gentleman claims to have been misrepresented?
Mr SINCLAIR-Yes, Mr Speaker. Mr SPEAKER-'-He may proceed. Mr SINCLAIR-The Sydney Morning Herald of 4 May made the following comment:
Mr Ian Sinclair is refusing to put on oath his denial that he knew Australia continued to breach an international trade agreement by selling cheap milk powlier.
On 27 February 1981 I received a letter from Senator Peter Rae, Chairman of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations, in which he set down particulars of some evidence received by that Committee and asked for my comments on it. I responded to that letter on 16 March and then on 22 April 1981. I further reponded in the following way:

Dear Senator Rae, The letter under your hand as Chairman of the Senate
Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations dated 25th March, 1981, has been received.
My response to your earlier correspondence of the 16th March, 1981, was written as a Minister in accordance with the several Oaths taken relating to that office.
My comments were based on my own recollection and supported by Departmental records and my Ministerial diaries.
In my letter of 16 March 1981 I set down in considerable detail my response to Senator Rae's letter which was dated 27 February 1981. As my letter stated, the detail was in accordance with my own recollection and was written as a Minister of the Crown. I have now received a further letter from Senator Rae dated 6 May 1981 in which he comments, inter alia:
Nevertheless, we are left with the dilemma that sworn evidence was given to the Committee which is contradicted only by an unsworn letter from you.
In your letter dated 22 April 1981 you stated the written evidence you gave to the Committee by letter dated 16 March 1981 was given as a 'Minister in accordance with the several Oaths taken relating to that office'.
From our examination of the oaths taken by Ministers we are unable to see any declaration that would place written evidence given by a Minister on the same basis as sworn evidence.
I do not believe it appropriate that a Minister of this House should appear and give sworn evidence before a committee of the other House. Therefore, I make this statement with respect to the evidence that I have tendered to that Committee in the Parliament and, of course, in accordance with my responsibilities to this Parliament. I believe that is the correct and proper form by which I can respond to the honourable senator. I assert to that honourable gentleman and to the members of the Senate Committee that I believe the evidence given to them is totally correct and has the substance of my being a Minister and giving it in this place. I do not believe there is any reason for a statutory declaration to be given to confirm formal correspondence entered into by me as a Minister in this place.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford Smith)-Mr Speaker, I seek your indulgence. I do not wish to cast any reflection upon the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair). Often it is very difficult for Senate committees to understand that they cannot get evidence from Ministers of the Crown. It would be in the. interests of the Parliament and the Minister if he made a statement indicating where the evidence given is in conflict and why. Then everybody in both Houses would know the position.
Mr SPEAKER-No doubt that proposal has been heard by the right honourable gentleman. I

GRIEVANCE DEBAT.E
35-Hour Working Week-Public Service: Staff Ceilings-Passports-Campbelltown City
Council-Telephone Service in Shoalhaven Area
Question proposed:
That grievances be noted.
Mr COHEN (Robertson) (12.14)-A few weeks ago this House was debating the 35-hour week. Unfortunately I was not able to speak in that debate. Today I want to suggest to the House a way in which the 35-hour week can be introduced into at least one part of the economy to the satisfaction of the whole communityemployers, workers and consumers. I ask the honourable members to listen patiently to what I have to say and not to close their minds and reject the proposals until they have considered them carefully. I want to talk about one sector of the economy-retailing, because that is one area with which I am fully familiar. The House is aware that for over 20 years I have been involved in retailing in men's and ladies' fashion on the North Shore of Sydney. Before becoming a member of Parliament I was the full time manager of a business and since 1969 I have retained my interest in that business; so I am sure that the House will accept that I have sufficient experience to speak with some authority.
I am sure that everyone would be aware of the controversy that has surrounded not only the question of the 35-hour week but also the question of extended shopping hours, in particular, Saturday afternoon shopping. What surprises me in this debate is that the proposals I am about to put forward have not, to the best of my knowledge, been suggested before. Let me first of all summarise the different views of the three groups involved in the debate. The attitude of employers varies. One view is that they should be allowed to trade when they wish and if they could have extended shopping hours the consumers would

spend more, thus increasing the general prosperity. Consumer spending has been sluggish in recent years. The employers argue that if consumers had extended shopping hours, particularly on the weekend, they would spend more. The argument that retailers wish to open only to give better service is in my view nonsense. The reason--there should be no need to apologise for that--is that they would hope to sell more. Undoubtedly, consumers would get better service with extended shopping hours, but that surely is not the motive. Increased turnover and profitability is a perfectly legitimate goal in a free enterprise society and retailers should not be coy about saying so.
Other retailers argue against extended shopping hours because they claim that the consumer has so much to spend irrespective of the time available. They argue that opening their shops for longer periods would simply increase overheads, particularly wages, without any commensurate increase in turnover. The result, they believe, would be reduced profitability and reduced leisure, particularly for small shop keepers who incidentally provide the vast majority of retail outlets. To maintain profitability they would have to increase margins, thus passing the costs on to the consumer.
Let us look at extended shopping hours from an employee's point of view. The average retail shop is open from 9 a.m. to 5.30 p.m., five days a week, one evening a week from 5.30 p.m. to 9 p.m. and Saturday morning from 8.30 a.m. until 12 noon although conditions vary from State to State and within States. Shops are open for almost 50 hours. Shop assistants work a 40-hour week. They also rarely ever get a full weekend off. Unlike the rest of the community, they are unable to go away for a weekend. That is the great disadvantage that most retail employees have.
From the consumers' point of view, the majority of the work force is at work at the same time the shops are open. With the exception of their lunch hour, Saturday morning and one evening a week the opportunity to shop leisurely is severely restricted. For most people whose place of work is a distance from shopping centres, the only opportunity to shop is on Saturday morning or one evening a week. These are the times when shops are crowded and shopping becomes a very tortuous experience. The consumers rightly ask the question: 'Why can't shops be open at times when we are not at work?' This debate has gone on in this country for decades without any resolution. Some years ago one night a week shopping was

2112 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Grievance Debate

introduced in most States and this has at least relieved some of the pressure that occurred on Saturday morning.
I recently took out some figures from my own business to ascertain the pattern of retail shopping hours for men's and ladies' wear. The figures were taken over many months and it was fascinating to observe the incredible consistency in the figures. Let me run through the men's wear figures: Monday 12 per cent, Tuesday 8 per cent, Wednesday 11 per cent, Thursday 26 per cent, Friday 14 per cent and Saturday 29 per cent. There are only 3t hours for shopping on Saturday. The figures for ladies' wear are: Monday 7 per cent, Tuesday 17 per cent, Wednesday 15 per cent, Thursday 25 per cent, Friday 19 per cent and Saturday 17 per cent. The important point is that the figures for Thursday were accounted for during the period between 6 p.m. and 9 p.m. In other words, 61 per cent of women's wear and 69 per cent of men's wear was sold on Thursday, Thursday evening, Friday and Saturday morning. A more detailed analysis revealed that approximately 40 per cent of women's wear and 50 per cent of men's wear was purchased on Thursday night and Saturday morning; in other words, 50 per cent of the turnover was done in 10 per cent of the shopping hour time available-the hours when shops were open and factories were closed.
I am fully aware that men's clothing would probably be one of the most sought after goods in the after-hours period. Friday, and to a lesser degree, Saturday are the big food shopping days. However, from discussions I have had with many other retailers who sell different merchandise I can assure the House that most stores take between 30 per cent and 50 per cent of their turnover during those five to six extra hours on Thursday night and Saturday morning. My experience is solely in suburban stores. We must be conscious of the fact that there has been and will continue to be a major shift in retail shopping from the central business districts in the cities to the suburban shopping centres as occurred during the 1960s and 1970s:
If I have been a little long-winded in coming to my suggested changes please forgive me, but it is important to understand fully the background to what I propose. My proposition is that retail shops should open until 4 p.m. on Saturday afternoon and close all day Monday and that on Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday, Friday and Saturday mornings they open at 9.30 a.m. Little or no business is done in the first hour that shops are open.
Let me now detail the benefits to all sectors of the community. In the case of the employers,

there is a slight reduction in overheads and also increased leisure. That is important for small shop keepers. The big advantages, however, for small shop keepers would be the Monday when warehouses, offices, banks would be open and their shops closed. It is a major problem for most small shop keepers to find the time to study properly ranges of goods and to see their accountants, their bank managers and their suppliers without spending valuable time away from their shops. A full day to do the 101 chores that need to be done outside the store would be a godsend to most small retailers.
In the case of the employees, the benefits would be enormous. Every weekend would be a full two days off, enabling them to go away if they wished from late Saturday afternoon at 4 o'clock until Tuesday morning. The working week could be 35 hours without any problem. The greatest advantage to employees would be the convenience of enjoying the beach, sporting facilities, restaurants and tourist resorts on Monday when they are not as crowded as they normally are on Saturday and Sunday. This would also be mutually beneficial to the recreation industry which finds Monday a very quiet day.
One further idea that might be feasible-it has been suggested to me-is the idea that the central business district, that is, the city proper which I am told has suffered a very profound fall-off in Saturday morning shopping since the shift to the suburbs during the past 20 years, should not open at all on Saturday but remain open on Monday. This then would enable suburban shop assistants and employers to shop individually or as a family in the city. So, we would have, in fact, six days for shopping but a different five days in the city proper to the five in the suburbs.
Finally, let us look at the advantages to the consumer. The consumer would gain an extra four hours shopping on Saturday afternoon. This may not appear much, but on the figures that I provided earlier, illustrating that between 60 per cent and 70 per cent of retail shopping is done on Thursday evening, Friday and Saturday morning, the extra four hours, is, in effect, an increase of 33 per cent 011 the peak shopping period. I have given this matter much thought over many years. I have discussed it with shop assistants, retailers and customers. I have always found that 99 per cent of people agree and say: 'Well, why hasn't it been done? It seems the sensible thing to do'. I ask that this at least be given serious consideration and that the matter at least be the subject of discussion and debate. Why not at least try it for a trial period?

Grievance Debate

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2113

Finally, let me say that 1 disagree completely with the suggestion that the Government proposed last week to deregulate completely shopping hours in Canberra. We need regulation to prevent overheads soaring, increased costs, reduced leisure, both to the employee and the employer-I might add that I know what it is like never to have a weekend off-and the exploitation of shop assistants. It is unfortunate that that does occur in some cases. The idea that the consumer should be able to buy something 24 hours a day, seven days a week, is nonsense and unrealistic. Let us give the consumer a better deal with Saturday afternoon shopping, the shop assistants 35 hours a week, a full weekend off every week and the retailer more profitability and more leisure.
Mr ADERMANN (Fisher) (12.24)-Last week in this House the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) made a statement on actions proposed pursuant to the deliberations and investigations of the committee for the Review of Commonwealth Functions. I accept that the result sought is economic growth, more jobs, higher incomes and a better life for all. I accept too the commitment to smaller government and the view that the Government should avoid excessive intrusion into the individual's rights and opportunities to make his own choices and set his own goals and priorities. I welcome too the philosophy of returning to State and local government responsibilities and administration which properly ought to repose there, and a commencement on the removal of the wasteful, inefficient duplication and overlap. I too enjoin the States to address themselves to a similar review, because restraint, efficiency and resistance of bureaucratic topheaviness is no less necessary in State government and could produce considerable benefits.
One of the discernible consequences of the Whitlam Labor Government was an explosive growth in the public sector with remarkable growth in departments and in the Public Service. The legacy of those policies in instability, destruction of confidence in the private sector, inflation and unemployment have been clearly seen and many times clearly exposed in the Parliament. Thus the restraints in staff ceilings and the actions taken by the Government to contain this bureaucratic monolith have been well intentioned. The Prime Minister has indicated that the reduction or abolition of functions and changes outlined could lead to a total reduction in staffing of 16,000 to 17,000.
I want to comment on that aspect and highlight some very real concern that I feel not about the goal but about the progress to that goal. The

growth in the Public Service was observed at all levels, all divisions and all classifications. New departments had emerged with their traditional pyramid of power and personnel. It has not only been a growth in clerks and officers at the lower level but also a growth in the Second Division in assistant secretaries, first assistant secretaries and the whole range of positions. At these higher levels, each with its own service staff, secretaries, clerks and advisers, the growth has been equally discerned. But I am most concerned that when staff ceilings are set and retrenchments are made so often the cuts and retrenchments are at the lower end of the scale. The top half of the pyramid, which determines the numbers in the lower echelon who should be dispensed with, is relatively undisturbed. The structure remains and the basis exists and can proliferate again if the Government relaxes its restraint.
What is the result which unfortunately manifests itself in a purely arbitrary reduction in staff ceilings? Unfortunately, for all the Government's intention otherwise, it can be service and help to the public which suffer. At the lower level we can see fewer personnel in local social security offices and Commonwealth Employment Service offices and fewer nursing staff in repatriation hospitals-in short, at the level where services and assistance are dispensed to the public and to the taxpayers who provide the salaries of the Public Service. This does happen and I believe it has happened. Higher up the scale we hear complaints that staff ceilings are preventing provision of information and services, yet the peaceful waters at those levels often do not exhibit a ripple.
I agree with the Government's aims to contain and reduce the public sector, and I realise that unless we maintain that course we cannot hope to obtain economic stability and give the private sector room to move, to grow and to provide jobs. But the Government cannot sit back and say that its aim of 16,000 or 17,000 fewer public servants will be attained-or, as the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) is reported as saying 16,000 or 17,000 fewer snouts at the trough-and just hope that the Public Service will reorganise itself into that new structure. My concern is where and how the reductions will be achieved. Will they be only or almost entirely at the lower levels or will there be very close supervision which will ensure that we will not achieve a smaller though very top-heavy and inefficient structure? Will the people who rely on the services and who should benefit from the reduction in bureaucratic growth and controls suffer frustration, reduced effiencies and services?
