From my inquiries it appears that there is some discrepancy between the statements emanating from the passport office of the Department of Foreign Affairs and those emanating from the clients. According to the Department, the current time for applications to be processed and issued is something like two weeks for applications made across the counter and about 25 days for application made by mail. According to the experience of travel agents and people who have come to see

The Government has put a sound proposition me a five week delay would be considered a short

to Parliament. The review has been thorough and period. That is not two weeks. This delay does

Grievance Debate

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2115

affect a large number of people who are planning trips. One agent indicated that the norm for the issuance of a passport would be closer to eight weeks. In fact, the other day, I received word from a person who had to wait nine weeks to be issued with a passport. Of course, this is something which is quite intolerable.
There appears to have been a breakdown in the system somewhere. A lot of people, many of whom have not travelled before and who were looking forward to their trips, have developed considerable anxiety and have experienced disruption not only to their plans for leaving this country but also to the plans the'y have made for visits to other countries. In fact, I heard of one instance the other day of one intending traveller having to cancel his trip as a result of these delays. Many of the people affected include those intending to visit families overseas and, as I said, people conducting business and people who are going on well-earned trips, many of whom have waited for many years until they retired to make those trips. Some of these trips have been aborted, truncated or in some ways upset. It IS difficult to have to postpone a flight. People who book on advance purchase excursion fares find themselves inconvenienced. Some people have had to shorten their journeys or, as I said, cancel them because of what would appear to be a process of neglect. I do not know whether this process is deliberate but, certainly, it is a process of neglect by the Government in this crucial area of the Public Service.
Some direct effects relate to 1981, but certainly not just to 1981. I think one has to look at an earlier period to see where the rot began. Early in 1981 a new form was introduced for completion by passport applicants. This again caused considerable concern to people. I know this from personal experience. People came to my office requesting me to complete their forms because they were concerned about the complexity of the form. At the same time a new machine was introduced in the passport office supposedly to streamline the process. But the personnel issuing the passports were not familiar with how the machine operated. This slowed down the process. Then the normal seasonal fall-off in demand for passports over the Christmas period did not occur in 1980. So, the normal catch-up period did not eventuate.
The culmination of those factors led to a serious backlog in the processing of applications. Until 1975 the processing of passport applications was handled by the Department of Immigration. Thereafter that matter was taken over by the Department of Foreign Affairs. The demand for passports since 1975 has risen by 50 per cent. But

the Department of Foreign Affairs still has the same number of staff, to a large degree, handling applications in the same way as the Department of Immigration did at that time. In response to numerous complaints and a submission by the Department of Foreign Affairs-God bless the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) who is sitting at the table-the Public Service Board provided some additional staff in Melbourne and Sydney. I understand that IS additional staff were provided in Melbourne and six additional staff were provided in Sydney. However, these are temporary staff. No new permanent staff have been allocated. The temporary staff can be expected to leave when the demand falls off.
The Government's decision concerning the requirements [or travellers to and from New Zealand to hold passports or visas is expected to lead to a new rush on passport applications when that provision comes into effect in July this year. I ask honourable members to bear in mind the backlog of applications which already exists. The Department of Foreign Affairs has said that the situation has improved significantly. I have already referred to the discrepancies between the stories of the Department and the clients. I have been putting the stories of the clients but, of course, we must consider the Department's story also. The Department says that the situation has improved significantly and the waiting time for passports is now two weeks only for over-thecounter applications and 25 days for those applications made by mail.
It is always unfortunate, I think, when people have to stand up in this House and berate the Government. Yet, because of events of recent days one has to bring to the attention of the people of this country some of the underlying causes of this sort of neglect. Perhaps we have seen the culmination of the Government's pursuit of a particular ideology over the last week or fortnight. Action taken in 1975 and measures introduced since then have led to the present situation. The high priests of this neo-Benthamite religion of Milton Friedman, so liberally spread as they are through this Government they are spread all over the place this morning-intone their monotonous and somwhat monstrous incantations with mesmeric effects upon their fellows. That is evident from what took place this morning. The principal symbol of their religion is small government. Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (12,43)-The honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) and I have today issued a joint statement condemning the brutal bashing of the town planner of the Campbelltown City Council by standover thugs wh? are trying to force the Council to give up its action to prevent a brothel from operating in Iolanthe Street, Campbelltown. The mayor of Campbelltown and aldermen-my friends and colleagues right across the political spectrumhave been subjected to continuing telephone threats by criminals involved in the brothel business. I warn these thugs that any attempt to threaten democratically elected representatives in this way strikes at the very heart of the democratic process and that the people of Australia will not tolerate it. None of us elected to represent the people of this area will bow to this kind of thuggery.
In the minute remaining I say briefly that tomorrow at 6 a.m., 12,000 subscribers in the Shoalhaven telephone area will gain full access to the subscriber trunk dialling service of the Australian telephone system when the new $3m Nowra telephone exchange begins operations. I am overjoyed that this is taking place at this time because there was a time when some pressure existed for this project to be deferred. I am very grateful indeed to two former Ministers for Telecommunications, the late Eric Robinson and the now retired Tony Staley, and the former chairman of Telecom Australia, Mr Bob Somervaille, who agreed to my strong representations that this project go ahead earlier than scheduled. I am grateful because Nowra now has full access to the STD service instead of having, as I understand it, the oldest telephone exchange of any large city in Australia. I am grateful to Telecom for all the work it has done. I am grateful particularly to the people who had charge of providing a service under the old and very tired exchange systemthe Telecom operators, the technicians and the trunk call operators in the exchange. For years they have struggled to provide an adequate service to the people ofmy electorate.

VOTING RIGHTS FOR MIGRANTS -!. ~ - Ministerial Statement
Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs)-by leave-I take .this opportunity to report to the House on discussions with State and Northern Territory Ministers about the eligibility for the franchise of new settlers. This matter has been discussed among Ministers responsible for immigration and ethnic affairs matters regularly for the past two years or so. At our last meeting which was held on 14 and 15 April 1981 agreement was reached on the following:
(1) Migrants, irrespective of their country of origin should be treated equally in relation to requirements to enrol, on their right to vote at elections for parliament and to nominate as a candidate for election to parliament;
(2) Australian citizenship is the appropriate basis for the franchise;
(3) No person currently enrolled as an elector should be disenfranchised;
(4) Uniform legislation to give effect to the foregoing should be enacted by the Commonwealth and all States.
In addition, all Ministers agreed that it would be appropriate to introduce the new arrangements from 1 January 1982 but this is subject to confirmation by respective governments.
Honourable members will be aware that under existing legislation British subjects may enrol and vote after six months residence in \ustralia whereas other migrants must attain Australian citizenship-involving a minimum residence period of three years-before they are entitled to enrol and vote. In the interests of clarity, it should be noted that the term 'British subject' includes citizens of most former British colonies. T seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a list of 42 countries whose citizens are British subjects and entitled to vote under existing laws after six months residence in Australia. Honourable members will note that the list runs from the Bahamas and Bangladesh to Zambia and Zimbabwe. Of course, some former British colonies removed themselves from the Commonwealth and their people from British subject status but the majority remained and retained that status.
Leave granted.

Mr MACPHEE-The removal of the anomaly between British subjects and other migrants was recommended by the Report of the Review of Post-arrival Programs and Services for Migrants more widely known as the Galbally report. When this report was tabled in the Parliament in May 1978 the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced the Government's acceptance of all of its recommendations. The report in this respect stated:

I am informed that it is now used only by the United Kingdom and Australia and that the United Kingdom is moving to adopt other approaches. It is fitting, therefore, that Australian legislation be re-examined as well. In most countries, the primary condition for obtaining the franchise is citizenship. Following the Galbally report an interdepartmental committee examined and reported on voting rights. After examination of that report by Cabinet I discussed this question with my State and Territory counterparts at a conference of Ministers in Darwin in 1980. It was agreed at that conference that Ministers would seek the reactions of their respective Cabinets and report back at the next conference which was held in April 1981. At that conference last month, Ministers expressed strong support for the changes I have just foreshadowed. I emphasise that the changes will not, of themselves, confer a right to vote upon any

introduced all Australians were British subjects. That term described a common or shared status of citizenship of the Commonwealth of Nations. In the 1940s, each of these nations introduced its own citizenship but retained strong links with other Commonwealth nations by granting each of its citizens the status of a British subject. In Australia citizenship itself was more readily available to British subjects than to other settlers until 1973. Since then, all settlers have had to satisfy the same residence requirement for citizenship. We are now taking the next step and placing the franchise on an equal footing.

further categories of people. The only group of migrants affected by the changes will be British subjects-that is people who are not Australian citizens-who have not enrolled before the end of 1981. In future, British subjects will not be entitled to enrol unless they become Australian citizens. I should also emphasise that the changes will not disenfranchise anyone.

The most fundamental reflection of commitment to the nation is citizenship. The most fundamental expression of citizenship is the right to elect those who govern the nation. Against that background it is of interest to consider some statistics. In foreshadowing legislation to vary the entitlement of future migrants to enrol and to vote, I emphasise two things:
Firstly, all Australian residents who believe they qualify for Australian citizenship should enquire at the nearest office of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs or lodge an application; and, secondly, British subjects who believe they are entitled to enrol on the Commonwealth Electoral Roll should inquire at or make application to the Australian Electoral Office before the end of 1981.

Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (12.55)-The Opposition, as is warranted, treats the announcement by the Government with the seriousness that it deserves. We see the ramifications of the change and it is to be hoped that the changes enunciated by the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee) do not in any way offend those people already living in Australia. I think everyone in this country would agree that all people coming to live in Australia should be treated in exactly the same way. Whilst the Galbally Review of Post-arrival Programs and Services to Migrants may have recommended along those lines I do not know that it went as far as to say that citizenship ought to be the basis for the franchise. Nonetheless, that is a stand which the Australian Labor Party takes at the moment.
I point out at the outset that the policy put forward at the election in October last year by the Labor Party, expressed by my colleagues the honourable member for Maribyrnong (Dr Cass) who was then the shadow Minister and by the leader of the Labor Party, was that all migrants, irrespective of where they come from, should have citizenship for the purposes of the franchise but that the period after which they would become eligible to make application for citizenship should be reduced from three years to one year. I make it quite clear that in respect of the statement made by the Minister the Opposition has not changed its mind. The Opposition believes it is right that all migrants should be treated equally. In this case that means people coming from any

We in the Opposition see no major substantive argument for leaving the set period at three years. Indeed, according to the figures given by the Minister it appears that the period stipulated does not seem to make much difference to the migrants themselves. Those people who do not wish to take out citizenship do not seem to have been affected by the reduction from five years to three years. It may be the case that they will not be affected by a reduction in the period from three years to one year. Whilst it is the overwhelming view of the Labor Party at the moment that citizenship ought to be the basis for the franchise-certainly, it is a view I hold very strongly at a personal level - It IS not a view that goes unchallenged. As honourable members would be aware, views have been expressed by some members of the New South Wales Labor Party committee on ethnic affairsalthough action has not been taken by the New South Wales Government-that a mere 12 months residency should be the basis for a person's being allowed to vote in this country. People from other parts of Australia hold similar views. It may be that there are people in the Government parties who hold such views.
At the moment the policy we put forward at the last election stands as far as the Labor Party is concerned. If the Government has a substantive reason for continuing with the three-year residency period then such a reason is certainly not to be found in the Minister's statement. We cannot find any argument why that three-year period remains. I think it is quite a change not only to say to people who might be described as British subjects that in the future 'You will have to take out citizenship' but also to say The period to which the change relates will increase from six months to three years'. Again it seems to me that it would have been better to compromise the time period with that citizenship commitment. Perhaps the residency period then could have been reduced quite substantially. Certainly, a Labor government would look very closely, as soon as it came to office, at reducing the period for all people, not just British subjects, in terms of applications for citizenship in this country. I make those points in relation to where we stand.
I think it is also important to say in relation to the measure that agreement with the States, of course, has been sought but that again there are varying views within the States as to what they

Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (2.43)-1 wish to make some comments on the important statement that has been made by the Minister for Immi~ gration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee). The statement deals with the eligibility of new settlers in Australia to vote. For a long time different tests have applied to people coming from different parts of the world. That has been a matter of some concern to migrants. That concern is certainly appreciated by me. In the past those people who came from former British colonies were able to enrol in order to vote shortly after their arrival whereas those who came from other countries were not. The Minister has made it quite clear that migrants to Australia, wherever they come from, seeking to vote in Australian elections will be able to do so on the same basis, that is, when

they become citizens of Australia. I think that is a fair test.
There seemed to be some unanimity of view when the Opposition spokesman on this matter, the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young), addressed the House. Having heard some of the other Opposition speakers one could be forgiven for believing that that is no longer the case. The Government takes the view that citizenship is the appropriate test for people to be eligible to vote. The principal matter that has been called into question by the Opposition has been the length of time it might take a person to qualify for Australian citizenship. It ought to be clearly noted that Australia provides in its three-year eligibility test a very generous basis upon which people may seek to become Australian citizens. The honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) directed the attention of the House to the time it takes to acquire eligibility to apply for citizenship in other countries. That is important when we consider many of the countries whose former citizens come to Australia to settle. Quite frequently those countries set an inordinate time Which people who have taken up permanent residence have to wait before they can qualify for citizenship. In relation to some of the European countries, 10 years of continuous residence is required before migrants in Austria and in the Federal Republic of Germany qualify for citizenship. In Greece-a considerable number of new Australian residents and citizens are from Greece-it is required that the people seeking Greek citizenship spend eight of the 10 years preceding the application in Greece. In Italylikewise there are many Italians who have setled in Australia - five years' residence is required.
I do not wish in any way to appear racist in the matters I am mentioning. I do not believe it applies only to European countries. For instance, in Malaysia 10 years within the 12 years preceding the application-including the one year immediately preceding the date of application-is the period for which people of that country must have been resident before they seek citizenship. I could go on. These comparisons with other parts of the world indicate that the requirements in Australia are extraordinarily generous. But one ought to ask oneself: What do the migrants want? When we look at the figures we find that on average it takes eight to nine years for people who have taken up permanent residence to apply for Australian citizenship; that is, when they exercise their own choice. I do not wish to debate this matter at length.
In case it is thought that Australia, in taking the approach that citizenship ought to be the test, is

2126 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Voting Rights/or Migrants

The point was made by several people, including the honourable member for Hindmarsh, that somehow or other this proposition is taking away the rights from British subjects. It is not doing that at all. I have gone out of my way today to stress that we are not taking away from any person any entitlement that he or she now has. We are not taking away any franchise. One could argue, I suppose, that in prospect we are taking away from future migrants who are classified in those 42 countries as being British subjects something that they might have expected to enjoy. They will be well aware of that when they migrate. If that deters them from migrating I would be very surprised. If it does, so be it.
It has been argued by the Opposition that we produce equality by lowering the requirement for everyone to the British subject requirement of six months' residency. Then a compromise was advanced to make that time 12 months. I might say that on the argument advanced by the Opposition if we do that we are still taking something away from the British subjects. We are not, in reality, doing that at all. I ask the House to consider this matter: We have a highly complex system of government in Australia. It is not uncommon for us as members of the Federal Parliament to be assailed by people at meetings and elsewhere about matters which we know are within the purview of local government or State government. Indeed, the honourable member for Hindmarsh once raised a matter at Question Time, I recall, with my colleague the Minister for Education (Mr Fife) about a matter that was fairly and squarely within the realm of a State Minister.
If it is difficult enough for people born in Australia to understand the distribution of powers under our Constitution and if it is difficult enough for them to make judgments about where responsibility lies and how votes should be cast it certainly is difficult for persons coming into Australia, especially if they do not speak English when they arrive, to be able to cast their votes in an informed fashion. Despite the efforts now being made in respect of adult migrant education I honestly believe, and the Government certainly believes, it would be irresponsible to give to people, unused for the most part to a federal structure with a bicameral legislature and a proportional representation system in the Senate, the right to vote until they really have understood the nature of the institution. Let us remember too that many of them have come from nondemocratic societies.
Another point about the proposal is that it has been discussed fully by the State Ministers and me at two lengthy conferences in the past year. The

State Ministers and I reached agreement in principle. We recommended to our respective Cabinets what the changes ought to be. Each of those Cabinets, including the New South Wales Cabinet, came to the view reflected in my statement to the House today. Really, the Opposition would do well to take note of the fact that the eight governments-the Federal, the Northern Territory and the six States-are in agreement about this measure. I have undertaken to the State governments that when the Electoral Act is amended or when the draft of the amendment is available I will circulate it to the States and they will be able to provide for reciprocal legislation. In 1973 the Whitlam Government introduced changes to the Australian citizenship Act and made a three-year residence requirement for everyone, British subject or not. I would have thought that should have been welcomed then as a step in the right direction. I would have thought also most honourable members would have agreed that the principal privilege of citizenship is the right to vote. Whilst it can be argued that some of the great European civilisations certainly allowed residents to be very active leaders in their communities, it can be argued that if a resident can have a commitment is it too much to ask that that resident show the commitment by becoming a citizen? The Government believes the appropriate way is that citizenship should be the basis for franchise, that citizenship is the recognition of commitment to our country.
Finally, I refer to the question of whether we are depriving anyone of any benefits. I remind the House of the statistics that I gave about the number of persons from the United Kingdom who have been here for many years and who have not taken out citizenship. Many of them may now choose to do so when they recognise that the right to vote goes with it. Other studies will be made in the course of the work of the task force on citizenship to find out why people choose not to take out citizenship. In conjunction with the States we will be conducting a citizenship awareness raising program. This is not to get jingoistic and so forth but to encourage more people to make that final commitment and take out citizenship. We have a close system of consultation with the States. Just as I have been consulting the ethnic groups with the honourable member for Port Adelaide over the review of NUMAS we are consulting the ethnic groups themselves and the State Ministers at the moment about the proposal. The State Ministers and I have agreed to meet when I have proposals to put to them about citizenship so that all aspects of the policy, procedures and possible

Consumer Protection

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2127

legislative changes will be examined in conjunction with them. There is a'!great deal of consultation proceeding. I hope that the Opposition, having registered its view today, will reflect upon it and will seek to bring itself into line with the States, the Territory and the Federal Government.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

WOOL INDUSTRY AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.

Second Reading
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.10)-I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to amend the Wool Industry Act 1972. Honourable members will recall that previous amendments which became law on 23 May 1980 provided for wool grower levies in the Market Support Fund to be progressively refunded to wool growers as the credit balance in the Fund reached agreed satisfactory levels. The Market Support Fund was established in September 1974 to provide funds to operate the Wool Reserve Price Scheme. Since that time wool growers have contributed to the Fund through a 5 per cent levy on their gross sales value of shorn wool.
The 1980 amendments to the Act were introduced after the Government acceded to strong requests from the Wool Council of Australia to revolve the Fund. The views of all sectors of the industry were canvassed at the time and not all agreed with the Council's proposals. However, contributions to the Fund are made by wool growers and it is only right that the views of growers should be given primary consideration. Revolving the Fund means that repayments will be made to the wool growers who contributed the

2128 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Consumer Protection

money on a first in first out basis after the Fund has reached an agreed satisfactory level. The Act provides that the Minister for Primary Industry will, with the written agreement of the Wool Council of Australia, make a declaration specifying a period of contributions to be returned to wool growers.
The level of the Fund currently stands well above $400m. This is in excess of requirements to operate the Reserve Price Scheme and it isrealistic at this stage to consider refunding levies collected during the first year of the Fund's operation. The continued collection of the 5 per cent levy will enable the Fund to be maintained at the desired level, for its primary role of market support. Development of the administrative procedures has been a complex task and has, however, revealed a number of aspects of the Act which need to be modified to remove certain difficulties and anomalies before the refund scheme is implemented. While the overall administration of the refund scheme is to be in the hands of the Australian Wool Corporation, much of the detailed work will be performed by wool brokers and wool merchants who are registered with the Commissioner of Taxation as agents for the collection of wool tax. These firms are referred to in the Act as 'registered persons'.
It is clearly undesirable for the Corporation, which does not hold the necessary wool transaction records, to be burdened with the task of making refunds to individual growers where this task could otherwise be undertaken more simply and less expensively by registered persons.
Wool growers who sold their wooi to or through a registered person will normally receive their refunds through that same person; that is, the firm which collected the 5 per cent levy. The Act as it stands provides that where a registered person ceases to be registered, the Corporation will pay the refunds directly to the wool grower clients. To cater for the situation where the firm concerned has since gone out of business, it was provided that refunds to clients of that firm were to be made by the Corporation. Some firms have ceased to be registered persons for the purposes of refunds because they were absorbed in takeovers or because they became registered under a different name. It now appears that many successor firms are capable of making these refunds, but are precluded from doing so by the provisions of section 42K(7) of the Act, which requires the Wool Corporation to make refunds in situations of this nature. Again, it is undesirable that the Corporation be burdened with the cost intensive task of making refunds to individual growers

where this work could be performed automatically by firms wpich have 'inherited' these growers as clients. Accordingly, clause 7 of this Bill amends the Act to remedy this situation.
A further proposed amendment removes an anomaly created by wool growers entering into certain. types of forward selling transactions. Under the Act as it stands at present, a decision to refund 1974-75 Market Support Fund levy payments would mean that some growers who sold wool forward before shearing prior to 1974-75, and eventually paid the levy during 1974-75 after shearing, would not be eligible for a refund. Other growers, however, who sold forward in 1974-75 but did not pay the levy until the following year would be eligible for a refund in respect of 1974-75. This situation is inequitable and not consistent with the first-in-first-out principle for revolving the Fund. Clause 5 of the Bill corrects the anomaly be deeming the time of sale of wool to be that time when payment for the wool is made.
An amendment proposed by clause 4 of the Bill will enable the Corporation, and registered persons assisting the Corporation, to be reimbursed for administrative costs incurred before the formal declaration of a refund period. The Bill amends the Act to enable these costs, which could not otherwise be recouped, to be charged to the Market Support Fund.
A number of other amendments of the principal Act are considered desirable and timely and it is opportune to introduce them now. I will refer to each of them briefly. The Bill provides in clause 10 that a statement given to a wool grower claiming a refund should set out the findings of the Corporation regarding the facts of the claim as well as the reasons for the Corporation's decision. The Bill also provides for an increase in the membership, and in the quorum for a meeting, of the Australian Wool Testing Authority. (Quorum formed). Since a significant proportion of the wool clip is tested in stores controlled by private treaty merchants it is appropriate that their organisation be represented on the AWTA. Additionally, the Wool Council of Australia is of the view that, as growers pay for the bulk of wool testing, the Council should be directly represented on the Authority.
Honourable members will be aware that the Government has made a separate decision to sell the AWTA, but no time scale has yet been set. Nevertheless, it is still appropriate for the Government to provide for the Authority's continued administration. Accordingly, clause 14 of the Bill provides for representation by both the Wool Council and private treaty merchants. As a

Fish (Export Charge) Legislation

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2129

result of this amendment, all major industry organisations will be represented on the Australian Wool Testing Authority. They will thus have an input in consideration of the future sale of the Authority and a first hand association with the shaping of ongoing testing and certification of wool. Clause 16 of the Bill revises the provision regarding declaration of pecuniary interests by Corporation members. The revision is in harmony with the conclusions and recommendations of the Committee of Inquiry concerning Public Duty and Private Interest. All members of the Corporation are required to declare any pecuniary interests in matters being discussed or considered by a meeting of the Corporation, and for that declaration to be recorded in the minutes of the meeting. Icommend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.

To achieve the 50 per cent cost recovery, it is proposed that the following rates be recommended to the Governor-General for promulgationby regulation; rock 10bster-3.9 cents per kilogram; all other shellfish except oysters, squid, cuttlefish and octopus-2.5 cents per kilogram; all other fish except oysters in the shell or half shell-0.34 cents per kilogram; oysters in the shell or half shell-l cent per dozen. On current estimates of fish exports for 1981-82 these rates will yield approximately $lm in a full year. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.
FISH (EXPORT INSPECTION CHARGE) COLLECTION BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.

FISH (EXPORT INSPECTION CHARGE) BILL 1981

Second Reading Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.22)-1 move:

Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.
Second Reading Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.19)-1 move:
That the Billbe now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to introduce, as from 1 July 1981, charges to recoup part of the cost borne by the Commonwealth for inspecting fish for export. This is part of the Government's program to recover costs of providing export inspection for certain agricultural and fishery products. Export inspection is designed to ensure that products meet minimum standards of quality, hygiene and presentation when exported. In 1979 the Government introduced charging for meat, grain and wool inspection aimed at recovering approximately 50 per cent of the inspeCtion costs of the Commonwealth. The Government proposes that fees aimed at a similar level of recovery should be imposed for similar services provided by my Department to seafood exporters.
Turning to specifics in this Bill, honourable members will see that it provides for the recovery of fish .export inspection costs to be achieved through charges on fish inspected for export and for which an export permit has been issued under the Exports (Fish) Regulations. The Bill provides that the person to whom an export permit is issued under the Exports (Fish) Regulations is liable to pay the charge. Exemptions may be provided in the regulations.

That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill is related to the Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill 1981 which was just introduced. Its purpose is to provide for the collection of charges proposed in that Bill. It follows the provisions for the collection of levies in other export inspection ch~rge legislation. Specifically, provision is made for exporters to pay charges due to the Commonwealth for the inspection of fish for which an export permit has been issued under the Exports (Fish) Regulations. I commend the Bill to honourable members.

PLANT VARIETY RIGHTS BILL 1981 Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.
Second Reading Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.26)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to make possible the granting of proprietary rights, somewhat similar to a grant of patent rights, to people who breed or develop new plant varieties. Whilst this is a new area of legislation in Australia, similar type schemes are currently operating in Europe, the United Kingdom, South Africa, the United States of America, New Zealand, Japan and several other countries. The proposed Australian scheme will, on the advice of the Australian Agricultural Council, be restricted to horticultural, ornamental and other selected pasture and fodder species. The major field crops and annual pasture species will not be considered for registration under the scheme.
The scheme, as set out in the B.m, will permit a plant breeder, who has developed a new plant variety, to apply to a registration authority for the grant of a right to confer sole ownership of that variety on him. Such rights will permit plant breeders, or organisations employing plant breeders, to levy and collect royalties from people selling or producing new varieties registered under the scheme. I emphasise that the granting of a right does not imply that the variety is better than

an existing one, but simply, as with other patents, that it is different.
A major advall,fna'ge resulting from a plant variety rights scheme will be increased availability of new plant varieties to the Australian horticultural, agricultural and plant nursery industries, with consequential benefits to farmers and gardeners. There is evidence that, in the absence of rights, Australia has been denied access to some potentially valuable varieties of plants developed overseas. These varieties have ranged through pasture grasses, grape vines, and a number of horticultural crop and orchard plants.
Plant improvement by breeding is a long and costly process. Rights of ownership of a new variety, with royalties on sales, will compensate to some extent the costs incurred and will provide a stimulus to companies and private breeders to enter into long term breeding programs. Such additional plant breeding will complement the work being undertaken by government funded organisations.
The development of the proposed Australian plant variety rights scheme has extended over several years. It evolved through a series of technical consultations between representatives of the Commonwealth, the States, seed industry groups and the Nurserymen's Association. The legislation covering the operations of similar type schemes in other countries was also examined in detail. I add that the Australian Agricultural Council has been closely involved with the scheme since it was first proposed. The Council will have an essential and key role in the operation of the scheme and it has already contributed substantially to the principles embodied in the present Bill.
In briefthese principles are:
The scheme will not have the potential to cover all plant species. It will be restricted in its scope.
Participation in the scheme will be entirely voluntary. A breeder may choose whether to release a new variety without applying for protection.
The grant of a right will be given where a new variety can be clearly distinguished by one or more important characteristics from any other variety whose existence is a matter of common knowledge. These characteristics must be capable of precise recognition and description and the variety must be homogenous and stable in it characteristics-in other words, it must remain true to its description after repeated reproduction or propagation.

Plant Variety Rights Bill

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2131

Nothing in the Bill will prevent farmers or gardeners retaining seed of protected varieties for sowing of crops for sale for human and animal consumption. Protected varieties will also be freely available for research purposes and to plant breeders for use in breeding programs.
Protection of a right, once granted, will be the responsibility of the owner of the new variety, through the normal legal process.
There will be provision for appeals to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and for the protection of the public interest.
The Act will be administered by my Department in which a registrar of plant varieties will be appointed. In the initial stages, only a limited number of species will be eligible for registration. These species will be determined on the recommendation of the Australian Agricultural Council.
The whole question of plant variety rights in Australia has already aroused considerable controversy. This is not entirely surprising, as similar arguments of opposition to such schemes were put forward in the United States, Canada and other countries. Some of the arguments that have been raised in Australia are misleading and show a lack of understanding of what is intended. For instance, there have been claims that under the scheme it will be illegal to grow existing well known varieties. That is not true. There have even been claims that plant breeders will deliberately breed disease susceptible plants that will require massive inputs of pesticides and fertilisers to survive. That is a ridiculous argument.
Other arguments do raise important issues, but these generally are not related to matters within the scope of plant variety rights schemes. For example, the preservation of plant genetic resources for use in future breeding programs is certainly important. It has been recognised as such in Australia and in other countries. Gene banks, or seed collections, particularly for the major crops, are in existence in Australia and are expanding. The Australian Agricultural Council has under consideration ways and means of improving existing collections and establishing new ones. However, these matters are completely unrelated to plant variety rights.
One of the most common complaints is that a plant variety rights scheme may lead to takeovers of Australian seed firms by multinational chemical companies. There is no denying that take-over bids can occur in any business activity, including the seed industry. There is nothing unusual about that in a free enterprise system. I am confident,

however, that if any takeovers should occur in the Australian seed industry, they will not be as a direct result of plant variety rights legislation.
Safeguards are provided for in this Bill against monopoly control that may not be in the public interest. These include powers for the registration authority to have control over licences and to ensure reasonable supplies of varieties at reasonable prices. As I have already said, species will be included only with the agreement of the Australian Agricultural Council.
Because of the need to encourage a greater understanding of the intentions of a plant variety rights scheme, the Government has decided only to introduce this Bill at the present time, and not to proceed with it further in the Parliament until the Budget session. This will allow several months for interested people and organisations to study the details of the Bi Il, and will in turn lead to more informed public discussion and understanding.
It should be pointed out that in introducing the Bill, the Commonwealth is primarily carrying out the wishes of the States, as expressed in Agricultural Council. The legislation, as it now stands, accords with what has been envisaged by the Council. The Council will discuss the legislation in detail at its next meetil).g in August, by which time it should also have before it the views of interested parties. I believe the Bill opens up prospects for encouraging development of small plant breeding firms in Australia, and therefore, an increase in the number of new plant varieties available, and improvements in the efficiency of Australian plant industries. I commend the Bill to the House.
Mr Kerin--Mr Deputy Speaker, I seek your indulgence to address a few short questions to the Minister.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Dr Jenkins)-The honourable member has my indulgence to make a brief statement.
Mr KERIN (Werriwa)-~As the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) has already said, there has been considerable and significant controversy in the community about the question of plant variety rights. J welcome his statement that the legislation will lay on the table for quite a while and that it will not be discussed until the Budget session. In a few places the word 'unanimous' may have slipped out of the Minister's second reading speech. Can the Minister inform us whether the unanimous agreement of the States is still required before the Government proceeds with the legislation.

Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (3.36)-1 move:

This Bill, in terms both of its size and the range of matters it covers, may well be described as an omnibus measure to amend the income tax law. Three of the measures will give effect to policies foreshadowed in the policy speech of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) for the last election. These are: An immediate deduction for capital expenditure on the connection of a business property to mains electricity; a concessional deduction for the cost of insulating a taxpayer's first home; and an immediate deduction for capital expenditure on storage facilities for petroleum fuel. Also included are provisions to give effect to the decision, announced by me late last year, that tourist buses may qualify for the investment allowance with effect from 1 January 1976. The Bill

In its present form the Bill would apply to connections or upgradings effected under contracts entered into on or after 1 October 1980. In the light of representations to the effect that this would disadvantage people in rural areas who,

Last, but by no means least in importance, the Bill proposes amendments designed to provide a counter to various forms of tax avoidance. Trust stripping, expenditure recoupment schemes and exploitation of trading stock transfer provisions and of the tax-exempt status of certain bodies are
the avoidance areas covered. Tax Avoidance
I turn now to remaining provisions of the Bill designed to deal with a variety of income tax avoidance practices. With one qualification that I shall specify later, the amendments have all been foreshadowed in announcements I made last year

2134 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Income Tax Laws Amendment Bill

on 5 March, 24 June and 4 August and on 30 January this year. With the introduction of this Bill, all proposals for action against specific schemes of tax avoidance foreshadowed in announcements I have made will have b~n given legislative form. I also expect that, before the House rises for the winter recess, new general anti-avoidance provisions will be brought before it for consideration.
Section 36A Schemes The Bill proposes the amendments foreshadowed in my statement of 30 January 1981. These are designed to provide a counter to tax avoidance schemes that set out to exploit the special provisions of section 36A of the income tax law that apply to changes in interests in trading stock, such as changes that occur on the formation of a partnership. This exploitation takes place under arrangements whereby a person who has, for example, some cattle about to be sold or a crop ready to be harvested, enters into a partnership with a company controlled by the promoter of the scheme. The cattle or the ~rop, as the case may be, is transferred to the partnership for an amount roughly equivalent to market value. However, a special right of election available under section 36A is used so that the property is treated for tax purposes as having been transferred at the substantially lower tax value at which it would have been brought to account if the transfer had not taken place. The net result is that the former owner realises an amount close to market value of the property, but is taxed only on the substantially lower value. The amendments proposed will make an election under the section ineffective where the person who disposes of an interest in relevant property receives consideration in respect of the disposal that is substantially more than the amount that might reasonably have been expected if the property had been valued at the low tax value applicable as a result of the election. The consequence of the ineffectiveness of an election in these circumstances will be that the person will be treated as having disposed of the property at its market value. Transfers made in ordinary family or commercial dealing are specifically excluded from the scope of the amendments. These amendments will apply generally to changes in ownership or interests occurring after 30 January 1981. However, consistent with action taken previously by the Government to debar the carry-forward of paper losses resulting from avoidance schemes, the Bill will ban the carryforward from one year to another of such tax

losses resulting from earlier section 36A schemes, with first effect forJh~ income year 1980-81.
Trust Stripping Schemes Changes that I outlined on 5 March 1980 to deal with variants of the trust stripping arrangements against which remedial legislation was enacted early in 1979 are the next matter covered by the Bill. These arrangements, which sought to free from tax altogether the trading profits and other income of trusts, commonly entailed a specially introduced nominal beneficiary being made presently entitled to income of the trust that was to be stripped. By this means, the trustee was relieved of any tax liability in respect of the trust income. The arrangements were at the same time designed so that not only did the special beneficiary escape tax, but the family from which the income was diverted received the benefit of it in atax-free capital form.
The 1979 amendments are contained in section 100A of the Income Tax Assessment Act and operate to treat income to which a beneficiary is nominally made presently entitled under reimbursement arrangements of this type as income of the trust estate to which no beneficiary is presently entitled. The trustee is thus liable to be taxed on the income at the maximum rate of personal income tax, which for 1980-81 is 60 per cent. The variants of the trust-stripping arrangements with which this Bill deals exploit the exclusion from the 1979 amendments of income of a trust estate to which a beneficiary becomes presently entitled in the capacity of a trustee cif another trust estate. That exclusion was intended to ensure that section 100A would apply only in relation to the last link in a chain of distributions through interposed trusts, thereby removing any possibility of double taxation on income as it passed through the chain.
The amendments now proposed will continue the scheme of applying section lOOA to the last nominal beneficiary only, but in a way that aims to prevent the exploitation that has been experienced. For this purpose, the exclusion from section lOOA of income to which a trusteebeneficiary becomes presently entitled is to be limited to so much of that income as is passed on by the trustee as income to which a beneficiary of that trust estate is in turn presently entitled. Further amendments will deny deductions for losses or outgoings incurred under arrangements to which the section applies. This will prevent the section being frustrated through such amounts being available for write-off against other income of the nominal beneficiaries. The amendments

In my statement of 4 August 1980 I foreshadowed action to extend the provisions to deal with variants of the recoupment type schemes that had by then been encountered. These involve paper losses generated in producing or marketing films, in operating gold mines, in purchasing consumable supplies and in carrying out market research. The amendments in respect of these variants are to apply with respect to losses or outgoings incurred after 24 September 1978. The decision to do this follows the clear warning given on that day and repeated subsequently.

In a statement on 24 June 1980, I foreshadowed legislation on tax avoidance through attempts to exploit the tax-exempt status of various entities. As I said at the time, two earlier warnings about the abuse of the privileged status of tax-exempt bodies had gone unheeded, and there was clear evidence that substantial revenue losses were accruing through the use of these bodies as vehicles for diverting taxable income from individuals and companies. I outlined in my statement examples of the kinds of schemes being employed. I do not think it is necessary to describe them in detail again now.

I now make public that more recent infor-

mation coming from returns of income for 1979-80 indicates that, before my announcement on 4 August 1980, still further schemes of the expenditure recoupment type had been implemented. Broadly, however, the schemes involved the diversion of income from a person who would otherwise be liable to tax on it to a body that has no tax liability. The exempt body was required to pay to the person from whom the income was diverted, or to an associate of that person, a tax free consideration equal to the greater part of the diverted income. As a fee for its participation it retained a small amount of the income for its own benefit.

Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (4.6)-1 move:

proper compliance with the Customs Act. Clause The purpose of this Bill is to amend section 114

13 proposes an amendment to section 64 to imthof the Excise Act 1901 to extend the validity

orise regulations to prescribe the time and manner period of Excise Tariff Proposals from six months

2138 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Health (Pharmaceutical Benefits) Bill

to 12 months. This amendment is being proposed as a consequence of the Customs Amendment Bill 1981. That Bill similarly proposes an extension to the validity period of Customs Tariff Proposals. Excise Tariff Proposals are introduced into the Parliament from time to time following a Government decision to alter excise duty. It has been the practice to incorporate the Tariff Proposals into an Excise Tariff Amendment Bill. These are normally introduced late in a parliamentary sitting. When an Excise Tariff Amendment Bill is unable to achieve passage prior to six months after a proposal is introduced, it has also been the practice to validate that proposal by an Excise Tariff Validation Bill. This proposed amendment will have the effect of reducing the number of Excise Tariff Bills. It will not, however, diminish in any way, the authority of the Parliament to scrutinise excise tariff changes as they occur. I commend the Bill to the House.

Bill (on motion by Mr MacKellar) read a third time.

The National Health (Pharmaceutical Benefits) Bill 1981, which was passed by this House on Thursday of last week, provided that the proposed Pharmaceutical Benefits Remuneration Tribunal would, at intervals of not more than 12 months, hold an inquiry into the rate of remuneration paid to chemists for the supply of pharmaceutical benefits. Our colleagues in another place queried whether holding an inquiry meant immediately handing down the findings flowing from that inquiry. Accordingly, our colleagues have amended this Bill to change the emphasis from holding an inquiry at no more than 12monthly intervals to making it mandatory to hand down the findings of an inquiry into remuneration at no more than 12-monthly intervals.
The Government supports the amendment passed by the Senate as it puts beyond any doubt the Government's intention that no more than 12 months should elapse between reviews of chemists' remuneration, including the publication of the results of those reviews. I reiterate for the benefits of honourable members that has it has always been of concern to me that under the present joint committee frameworks inquiries tended to be prolonged to the detriment of chemists. 1 commend the amendments to the Committee.

senators. Indeed, it may have bought off some of Bills. However, I indicate also that the Opposition

2140 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Bill

is not entirely satisfied with the assistance arrangements in respect of the textiles, clothing and footwear industries which have been adopted by the Government, and of which these three Bills form an important part.
I
Levels and forms of industry assistance are now among the most important economic issues facing Australia. The Opposition shares the view expressed in the report of the Crawford Study Group on Structural Adjustment which recommended that levels of protection afforded Australian industry should be reduced in the long term. However, we do not believe that sudden or dramatic reductions in protection are warranted, particularly at a time of high unemployment such as presently exists in Australia. While the rate of unemployment is at least 6 per cent of the work force, there is no logic in reducing protection so as to throw even more people out of work. I am sure that even this heartless Government would agree with those sentiments.

this Government there is market growth over the

period.

W j;

The Opposition recognises that the main benefit of a base quota allocation is stability for most of the importing industry. We recognise also that sale of quota maximises the efficiency of the importing sector and at the same time the auction system ensures that the maximum feasible revenue is gained by the Government rather than by private profiteers. While recognising that there is a trade-off between stability and the benefits of quota sale, it nevertheless seems unfortunate that the profits to be gained by those enjoying base quota allocations will always be greater than those to be gained under sale quota, assuming, of course, that the duty rate bids for sale quota reflect the high, effective rates of protection which import quotas convey. The duty rate on base quota is to be fixed at 50 per cent, whereas sale quota could well involve rates two or even three times this level.

The Opposition's commitment to the textiles, clothing and footwear industries is based on the realisation that the continued employment of about 120,000 Australians in these industries is essential. Many of these jobs are provided in regional centres with few alternative job opportunities. Many of the jobs are held by workers who lack the skills appropriate to alternative areas of industry. To reduce employment even more dramatically than is already occurring is not to achieve a restructing of industry. Rather it is to create more massive unemployment and to concentrate it in certain regions. We cannot countenance that as a proposition.
Having acknowledged the need at this time to maintain protection levels, it is also important to determine which are the most appropriate forms of assistance. We in the Opposition happen to believe that import quotas are appropriate in the case of certain industries, and the textiles, clothing and footwear industries are among these. It is perfectly clear that only quotas can provide the needed degree of certainty to the industry to enable it confidently to invest in plant which will help it produce a more competitive product in the future.
These Bills provide for the administration of import quotas on a two-tiered basis. Most of the quota is to be allocated to bona fide importers who have been involved in importing in recent years. However, a small proportion is to be sold by tender. Because the basic quota is to be fixed for the next seven years, the amount to be sold by tender will increase, assuming that even under

The sale by tender system will reflect the real protection afforded by the imposition of import quotas. It appears to the Opposition that the profits to be gained by this system should accrue to General Revenue as they would under a tariff system rather than to a privileged group of importers. Indeed, it is possible that, through strong bidding for sale quotas by base quota holders, these profits could be unfairly maximised.
These opportunities for profiteering should be overcome. As a minimum measure, the rate of duty on base quota should be reconsidered annually and adjusted in line with the trend of prices revealed by the tender system. The differential between base and sale quota duty rates should never be allowed to become excessive, if indeed it should be allowed to prevail at all. The recent and continuing revaluation of the Australian dollar upwards is causing an increasing disparity between the free on board price of imports and the price of locally produced products. In these circumstances, windfall profits are likely to be made by base quota holders. The Opposition is of the opinion that this should not be allowed to continue.
Indeed, this is only one area of concern to the Opposition. This problem is paralleled by the need to ensure that the/price levels on the Australian market are the lowest possible in respect of locally produced goods. This requires a greater degree of planning in the industry. The Opposition has long been of the view that complex and interrelated industries such as textiles, clothing and footwear should be given closer attention by

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Bill

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2141

government. It is not good enough to give an industry a level of protection which is possibly the highest in our history and then pretend that we can leave the rest to the market. History proves that market forces are not effective in this area.
We believe the Government has its head in the sand when it comes to the management of the industrial sector of the enconomy. On the one hand, at enormous cost it quite properly protects the private sector from the full impact of a free market environment but, on the other hand, it pretends to deregulate industry by irresponsible actions to remove proper and beneficial interventions such as price justification. We know, of course, what has happened to the Prices Justification Tribunal in the last few days. The Opposition believes that industry-specific policies are needed for industries such as these and that these policies should be appropriately devised and managed by industry authorities established for the purpose.
In addition to these general concerns about the administration of assistance arrangements for the textile, clothing and footwear industries, we have some particular concerns with the provisions of these Bills. We are particularly concerned that the arrangements for quotas do not allow a small number of large importers to tie up the market. Small firms must not be unreasonably excluded, and competition should be maintained. In this respect we hope that due consideration will be given by the Government to the call by Mr Clive Rodger of the Apparel Manufacturers Association of New South Wales that the security required under the legislation not be too high so as to be an overburden on small firms.
The Opposition has also been concerned that the right to buy and sell quotas is not abused and exploited by firms which are not bona fide importers. We understand that the officers of the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs will have stronger powers under the legislation in this respect. We are very happy about that. However, it is always essential that adequate staff is applied to these tasks. We call on the Government to ensure that its manic determination to bash the public sector does not result in costly losses in this area. In conclusion, I reiterate that the Opposition supports the Bills as a desirable improvement of the import quota system, notwithstanding its concern that there are a number of areas of the Government's assistance arrangements which are poorly conceived.

Mr John Dawkins Ref: to Clothing quotas
25 February 1981 Dear Sir,
Further to our discussion regarding looking into the possibility of increasing the amount of clothing we would need to import to retain a viable business.
As I explained to you, with the new quota system being introduced as of 1st January 1981. If our quota is based on our base performance for the two year period determined by the Federal Government, this would mean to us a complete loss of our wholesale business.
We have been importing clothing for more than five years, our total imports during this time have been small due to the lack of assistance given to us from Customs Tariff during this time. We were informed that certain items were dutiable, and as such did not import certain types of clothing such as rayon Batik. We were informed during 1975 to 1978 that all handicraft clothing must be made from natural materials such as cotton. We have now come to realise that Batik rayon is not dutiable, and we could have been importing rayon Batik without paying the duty as told before.
We have had difficulty finding suitable cotton material, and as such, were forced to import lower quantities than we had wanted at this time. During the last eighteen months, our imports of clothing have increased 1000 per cent, giving us the opportunity to develop our wholesale business.
We now employ one shop assistant plus my wife and myself, and were looking at employing more staff as our business

2142 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Bill

grew. If we are hit with quotas based on our base performance for the period determined by Federal Government, this would mean an end to our business as such.
During the five years I have been in business, we have worked seven days a week, sometimes a twenty hour day just to build our business to what it is today. I feel totally dissolutioned that overnight all the work we have put into our business can be undone through no fault of our own, just at a time when we can slow down to a 6 day week, 10 hour day.
We have built up a good clientel for our clothing during the last eighteen months, and now see everything we have worked for ending, just at a time when we should be receiving the reward we have worked for.
I have enclosed a list detailing clothing imported during the base performance determined by Federal Government, and sincerly hope you will look into this problem for me.
I sincerly hope you are able to help us in this matter, because without your help I do not know what we will do.
We have been informed that quotas will be made available for sale through auction. This will favour the large business that can afford large sums of money, and again enable them to control clothing prices without fear from small operators.
I feel this is another example of the small business going under to the benefit of the-large retail chain stores. I would like to ask what hope in this country has the small businessman when this unfair balance of power can put small operators out of business because they cannot front up with large amounts of money just to stay in business.
I only wish to be offered the chance to build up a business I understand, and at the same time offer employment to those that are willing to work.
I would like to thank you for taking the time to read this letter, and trust you will endeavour to help me in any way you are able.
Thanking you, Yours faithfully, PETER HOLDSWORTH

I want to read out two communications which I have received in the last couple of days. I mention briefly the decisions which have been nation's human capital for minimal financial savings.

made in the context of education as a result of the Lynch committee and to comment on the ways in which these decisions will seriously damage education in this country. In the first instance the Government has decided to re-impose fees for those students undertaking second or higher degrees in universities and colleges of advanced education. The fees will apply to those students who will enroll for the first time in 1982. The Government has decided that the fees should not apply to 'recognised double degree and diploma combinations. It has also decided that the fees will not be paid by those students in receipt of Commonwealth Government and university postgraduate awards. The scale of fees has not been announced because the Government has said that the level of fees will be set in the Budget context. The Government has said that there will be a reduction in the amount of funding to the colleges and universities as a result of its decision on the Tertiary Education Commission report to take account of the fee income which the universities and colleges might expect from the imposition of these fees.

In saying that the Monash University Staff Association simply affirms the remarks which I and the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) were making yesterday in another debate on a related matter. The Department of Economics of the University of Wollongong has also opposed the reintroduction of fees for second and higher degree students without a substantial expansion and upgrading of the Commonwealth Post-Graduate Award Scheme. It goes on to complain: about the way in which the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme will be tightened. I will come back to that in a moment. These complaints are not coming particularly from the students concerned, they are corning from the staff association in one instance and the academics from the Department of Economics in the other instance. The reason they are complaining is that they know the value of the contribution post-graduate students make to the research effort in universities and therefore to the research effort throughout Australia.

I think the point is that the Government, obviously, thinks that it will receive a fair amount of revenue as a result of the introduction of this measure. But inquiries and calculations that I have made show the Government is likely to receive not more than one per cent of the total amount which the Commonwealth spends on tertiary education as a result of the imposition of these fees. Not all post-graduate students will pay, but in addition many of those students who will be forced to pay will be discouraged from enrolling and, therefore, the Government will not receive fees from them. So, in return for imposing a measure damaging to Australia's research effort, highly disruptive to university education as a whole and highly disruptive to the administration of universities~these fees cannot be collected for nothing, as all that will be raised in the form of revenue is about one per cent of the amount that it currently spends on tertiary education.

I want to refer briefly to a survey which has just been completed at the Macquarie University; a university, by the way, which happens to be in the electorate of the Treasurer (Mr Howard). This is a preliminary paper. The survey was conducted only in the last couple of weeks. It indicated that 40 per cent of students would defer or withdraw from undergraduate education if they were forced to pay fees of even a fairly nominal amount of $300 a year. A survey was done in 1976 for the Education Research and Development Committee. That survey was supplemented by other surveys. What has come out of the Macquarie University survey in the last couple of weeks indicates that the resistance to paying fees is very much greater than was thought to be the case at that time. If fees of $1,000 had been imposed.on law students this year half of those students, who one would expect to go into professions with fairly high incomes after graduation, would not have enrolled in law school.

I refer quickly to the proposals that the Government has in mind in relation to the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme and loans. I do not want to go into all the ways in which TEAS has declined over the last few years; but we know that a declining number of people are receiving TEAS benefits and, particularly, that a declining number of people are receiving the maximum benefit. I did go into this matter in some detail in an earlier debate in this House. We cannot look at the loans scheme in isolation from TEAS, because the Government has stated clearly that from the time it introduces the student loans scheme it will set the benefits in relation to TEAS i.n the knowledge that extra supplementary assistance will be available through the loans scheme. There is absolutely no doubt as to what that means. The Government will either freeze the TEAS benefits or reduce them in order to force more and more people to take out loans to support themselves through tertiary education.

The Government has to recognise that the imposition of fees does provide a direct and severe deterrent to people to continue with their tertiary education. The fact that the Government is, at this time, imposing fees for post-graduate students and for second degree students means that this will severely disadvantage the most needy of those students. Clearly, the only assistance available in the form of post-graduate awards is awarded on the basis of merit and not on the basis of need. Therefore, students who are quite successful and
who can make a positive contribution will not be

This is at odds with a recommendation from the committee which the Government set up to inquire into the student loans scheme and which reported in 1977. That committee, the Butcher committee, said at the time that any loans scheme ought to be introduced purely as a supplement to the existing system of tertiary education assistance, that is, it did not say that a loans scheme should replace a tertiary allowance or even replace it in part. It said that there was a case for the introduction of a loans scheme on a supplementary basis for students who were unable to support

2146 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

States Grants (Schools Assistance) Bill

themselves entirely on the existing level of TEAS. That is not what the Government is saying on this occasion. It is forecasting that TEAS will be gutted. It will be available to fewer and fewer people. Clearly it will be available at a lower level of benefit than is the case at the moment. That will compel students to take out loans in order to pay their basic living costs.
There will be great resistance, particularly from students with poorer backgrounds, to incurring debts at this time of their lives. They are being asked to incur a debt at the age of 18 or 19 which they would pay back upon graduation. It is the Government's theory that once they graduate and obtain a job they ought to repay to society some of the investment which society has made in their education. It is a grossly unfair system in practise.
I refer again to the Macquarie University's survey made in the last couple of weeks. Four hundred and eleven students were asked how they predicted their ability to repay the loan. Only 6 per. cent said that they would certainly be able to repay it, 25 per cent said that most likely they would be able to repay it and something like two-thirds said either that it was unlikely that they would be able to repay the loan or that they would not be able to repay that loan. If that is what they thought, they would not take out a loan in the first place.
The reason they doubt their ability to repay the loan is that these students are aware of the high level of graduate unemployment that exists in this country. They are aware also of the fact that once they graduate in their early twenties that will be just the time when they will incur other debts for houses or cars or whatever. They do not want the extra burden of repaying the cost of having to support themselves through their university or college course. Repayment may be possible for those people who float straight into highly remunerative occupations upon graduation, but that will not be the experience of most gratuates. The people who will be most in need of extra support will be least likely to be attracted to the loans schemes.
I urge the Government to reconsider this matter. It is certainly not a cost saving device. In the short term it will cost the Government more as it makes available the funds for the loans scheme. It is true that over time some of the money will be repaid. The Government.expects that it will save, presumably by gutting the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme. But at the same time, this year and next year it will certainly cost the Government more. I suggest that the Government carefully reconsider the question of introducing a student loans scheme. It will discourage people

from entering tertiary education. I desperately hope that it is not .,the intention of the Government to discourgepeople from going on to higher education. Already we have appalling falling levels of participation in higher secondary and post secondary education in Australia. What we really ought to be attempting to do is increase the number of young people who are participating in education, not providing further serious impediments to their continued participation in education.
I refer to one other matter which relates to the area of schools education, that is, the quite absurd decision of the Government to close down the Curriculum Development Centre. The Curriculum Development Centre was established by~-
