The decision in respect of the Curriculum Development Centre is not a huge matter of national importance, but it does highlight very clearly the absurdity of many of the decisions in the education area which were made by the Government as a result of the Lynch committee. The Government has just spent $200,000 on refurbishing premises for the Curriculum Development Centre in Lyneham. It was only on 18 March, something like six weeks ago, that the current Minister for Education opened those new premises. He said that he was very pleased to have been invited to perform the official opening of the new premises. He then went on:
In the five years since its establishment the Curriculum Development Centre has undertaken a formidable array of projects which have resulted in an enrichment of the fabric of Australian education.
He further said:

States Grants -(Schools Assistance) Bill

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2147

Perhaps the highlight of the Centre's efforts was the release last year of the discussion paper entitled 'Core Curriculum for Australian Schools' which has been acclaimed both in Australia and internationally as an important contribution to this aspect of the education debate.
He went on:
I am sure that when public reaction to the paper has been assessed and analysed a refreshing approach to curriculum through a core curriculum concept will result.
This exercise is, however, by no means an isolated undertaking. In co-operation with the State education agencies, the Curriculum Development Centre has produced a most impressive array of curriculum materials of national importance.
Through its work the Centre has demonstrated to Australian educators, and others involved in education, the importance of on-going curriculum development, as well as the value of having identifiable areas of specific curriculum focus, as our education system strives to keep pace with rapid social change.
That is what the Minister thought just six weeks ago when he went over to turn the key at the CDC premises in Lyneham. He is to go back in a couple of weeks time to put up the shutters. That is a most deplorable turn-around. I would like to know what it is that has possessed the Minister to have such a complete reversal of attitude in just sixweeks.
I take the point of the honourable member for Dundas. I certainly hope the States will contribute to the costs of continuing the CDC although not because I think it is their obligation to do so. I do not think it is. This is a national task which costs the Commonwealth only about $2.5m a year. It is not an expensive undertaking, and already the States are making a contribution of just under $lm to the cost of running the place anyway. It is just a stupid decision of the Commonwealth. I hope that the States will come to the party and keep the centre going, though I might say that the chances of the States responding in that way would be much greater if the Commonwealth would turn round and hand over the $70m which, in my opinion, it deceitfully withheld the other week from the States.
I wish to raise one other matter and I refer to the quite short-sighted decision of the Government to restrict the activities of the Schools Commission. The Schools Commission has been making an invaluable contribution to the education debate in this country by the provision of information and the dissemination of that information. Through the activities of the Schools Commission we in this place and others around Australia have been able to make a more informed contribution to the education debate and have been more aware of the amount and direction of education expenditure in this country. The Government's decision to contract that activity is most unfortunate and undemocratic and will result in serious

problems for government and non-government schools.

Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (5.0)-1 support the Bills before the House, but before speaking in particular on the States Grants (Schools Assistance) Amendment Bill 1981 I seek the House's indulgence for a moment. I want to take this opportunity to thank the people of the Wentworth division for electing me as their representative in this historic House and also those hundreds of members and supporters of the Liberal Party who in the period from my pre-selection as candidate in March to my election as a member in April worked so hard in the campaign. The division of Wentworth is a Liberal Party stronghold in the eastern suburbs of Sydney. As some members of the Opposition frequently noted during the recent by-election, Wentworth has never been held by the Australian Labor Party, although nowadays, as a result of boundary changes, it has some booths where the Labor Party wins the support of a majority of voters. It is my intention as well as my duty to serve them all, regardless of party allegiance.
The division of Wentworth has always been well represented in this House by members who have played a major role in the national life of the country. As has been said by the honourable member for Wentworth and the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins), members of this House do not oppose the States Grants (Schools Assistance) Amendment Bill or the States Grants (Tertiary Education Assistance) Amendment Bill. In fact, they honour Government undertakings and, after all, that is a rare event in this House in recent times. So we can but approve them. I draw the attention of the House to the statement by the Minister for education (Mr Fife) about the Government's policy on education. On page 1862 of Hansard of 30 April, he is recorded as having said:
This is in conformity with the Government's resolve to ensure equality of opportunity and freedom of choice for all Australian school children and their parents.
I will go back to that statement later. I must admit that with regard to the schools assistance legislation I did have sympathy for the point of view put by the honourable member for Wentworth regarding the program to assist non-profit part time ethnic schools. These schools are well established in our communities and they do require assistance. Variations in standards are involved and I am pleased to see that this extra assistance will be paid to those schools where the-children are

undertaking approved supplementary community language and cult~ral education programs, because here there issoine degree of accountability. So, whilst I support these particular part time ethnic schools, I am concerned about the contribution that is made to multicultural education. It seems to me that although those schools satisfy the requirements of that one group in preserving the language, culture and knowledge of history of that group-a very laudable objective-they are still not breaking down the barriers between the culture of that group and those of other groups. Australia is now rich in ethnic people and ethnic culture. I would like to see the opportunity for multicultural exchange. This is not provided for in our schools at the moment in any effective form. The attempt in South Australia, with its program in a number of primary schools, does give some sort of lead but it has not gone very far.
I now move to the other program, which is to develop education programs for severely handicapped children. We are told the Bill makes provision for the introduction of a new scheme. What is the scheme and how will it operate? It will cost $19.3m but what is its objective and how will it be carried out? Just as an objective, it is acceptable. But are we not sensible enough in this Federal Parliament to. be told how it is to operate, what accountability there will be and what its objectives are? How far would it go? The Preston Institute of Technology had the Education Program for Infants and Children which dealt with Down's syndrome children. This was started under a special grant from the Department of Social Security. It was an early education program which began with babies affected by Down's syndrome in their first year of life. It was eminently successful an~ because of its success it .drew numbers beyond those approved for the pilot scheme. The effect snowballed and about three months ago the Institute thought it would not be financed beyond 30 June. Only recently has it been told it will be
allowed to carryon.
What is its future? What is the future for similar schemes under this education program for severely handicapped children, or does it not apply to those schemes at all? It seems to me that it is programs such as those for the severely handicapped which we should support. We are given insufficient information concerning them and the Government's objectives. Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (5.34)-I am glad to have the opportunity to congratulate the honourable member for Wentworth (Mr Coleman) on his maiden speech in this House. I am glad that iLwas on such an important topic as education. I believe his contribution to the debate was in keeping with the usual high standards that the Australian community has learnt to expect from the honourable member for Wentworth. T attended university with him in Sydney. I then worked with him in the late 1950s on the staff of a magazine called the Observer which ultimately merged with the Bulletin. The honourable member for Wentworth then became a most distinguished editor of the Bulletin magazine. I was very glad to work with him as the financial editor of that magazine for some years. I am overjoyed to see him as a member of this place and to hear the quality of the contribution that he has made to the debate on this important subject of education.

Schools Commission report for the triennium 1982-84 which was released last month. I draw the attention of the House to the fact that there has been a growth in the non-State system in recent times. There is no doubt about it. As a result of this Government's activities, there has been an increase in the number of enrolments and proportion of enrolments in the non-government primary school area following a degree of assistance given by this Government to that section of the education system. 1 point out that the recovery in that proportion, which is now 20 per cent of all enrolments, took place after a substantial fall in the number of enrolments in non-government schools which was generated under the Labor Government. In 1972, in the term of the previous coalition Government, the proportion of students who attended non-government schools in Australia at the primary level was 20 per cent. That is exactly the same as it was last year. In other words, there was a fall under Labor and

I will now deal in detail with the burden of the there has now been a recovery. We have restored

States Grants (Schools Assistance) Amendment a degree of choice in education which was being

Bill, particularly the Government's commitment diminished by the policies of the Labor Govern-

to provide over three years a total of $25m for ment. I think that point should be made.

States Grants (Schools Assistance) Bill

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2153

There has also been a significant increase in the resources available in the State system .and no doubt some increase inthose available to the nonState system in terms of the number of teachers. For example, in the time span I have just mentioned, from 1972 to 1980, there was an increase in the number of State school teachers of 35 per cent. The increase in the number of nongovernment school teachers was only 31 per cent. The State system, which it is alleged this Government has knocked down, has increased its number of teachers at a faster rate than 4as the non-State system even though the proportion of students attending school ,in the two systems is exactly the same now as it was in 1972. To pretend that this Government's policies have massively disadvantaged the State system as against the non-State system is evidently nonsensicaL
Let us look at what will happen in the future, There is no doubt that all the projections indicate a growing distaste within the community for the styles of some State schools and State school teachers. There is an increasing desire, evident in the projections, by people to exercise a greater freedom of choice to use other systems. In the S9hools Commission report the lower projection for the non-government primary school enrolments, which last year accounted for about 20 per cent of all enrolments, is an increase to 21.7 per cent by 1984. The higher projection is an increase to as much as 23.8 per cent, which means that the State schools would suffer an off-setting reduction. At the secondary level the same sort of trend is expected. In 1980 26.4 per cent of students attended non-government secondary schools. The lower projection is an increase to 27.3 per cent by 1984. The higher projection is an increase to 28.5 per cent, with off-setting reductions, of course, in the State school system.
Many people might ask: 'Why are people expressing this choice?' It could well be held that the more the people of Australia see of the activities of some teachers' federations around this country the more concerned they become about the level and quality of teaching and the attitudes of teachers in the State school system. The House probably does not need reminding that the New South Wales'Teachers Federation got solidly behind the producers of the recently controversial homosexual kit prepared which they hoped would be distributed in New South Wales schools. The Teachers Federation got so much behind it that it threatened to sue the Federal Government if the money for the kit was withdrawn. Of course, as a result, the Federal Government, regrettably, had to provide that money. This occurred despite the fact that approval for the provision of that money

was obtained by deceit. It is important to note that the Teachers Federation took a strong position requiring the Federal Government to provide the money for that homosexual kit even though approval for the money was obtained in a deceptive manner.
Let me dramatise further the disadvantage that the non-State school system has. I refer to student-teacher ratios. Once again, the position is evident from the Schools Commission report. The latest figures available are those in 1979. In government schools in the primary sector the student-teacher ratio was 20.3 per cent compared with 24.4 per cent in the Catholic school system. That indicates a massive disadvantage in the Catholic school system. Yet there are continual objections from honourable members opposite to our attempts to eliminate that disadvantage. The States are responsible for the State school system. We believe that we have an obligation to provide freedom of choice and equality of opportunity through the non-State system. Quite frankly, I would much rather the subsidies which at present are paid for each child under this Government's policy than the old system which relied much more heavily on a substantial taxation deduction. Under the old system the richer a person was the bigger deduction he got for having a child at a non-State schooL That system did not provide enough assistance to the Catholic education system in particular, which, as I have already outlined, caters to a large degree for the underprivileged and lower income groups in our community.
The disadvantage which I outlined in the primary school system also obtains in the secondary school system. In 1979 government schools had a pupil-teacher ratio of 12.3 to 1 but in Catholic schools on average there were 17 pupils per teacher. This trend is evident in the total staffpupil ratios in the government school systems too. In particular, I think it is worth pointing out the differences in the ways in which the various States run their education systems. It intrigues me when I hear many honourable members opposite and members of the New South Wales Parliament endeavouring to attack the Federal Government for a situation which is far from perfect in the New South Wales education system when in fact the Federal Government is obliged to adopt an even-handed approach across Australia, unless the States agree that it should not do so. Yet when we look at the New South Wales education system we find that its achievement levels under the Karme1 targets are way behind those of other States, particularly Victoria.

J seek leave of the House to incorporate in Hansard Question on Notice No. 43 and the very interesting answer given on 4 December 1980 by the Minister for Education (Mr Fife) under the heading 'Secondary and Tertiary Education'.

Leave granted.

The question and answer read as follows-

Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Education, upon
notice, on 26 November 1980:

(1) Is he able to say whether, in Australian secondary education, there is (a) a high drop-out rate of45 percent between
Years 10 and 12, (b) differing retention rates between Government, Roman Catholic and independent schools, (c) a variation in retention rates at year 12 between State systems,
e.g. 32.4 per cent in NSW and 24.3 per cent in Victoria, (d) a declining proportion of students proceeding from Year 12 to higher education of 54.6 per cent in 1974 and 48.7 per cent in
1977, and (e) a variation in urban educational participation compared to rural participation; if so, what estimates have been made of the percentage of young people aged between 17 and 24 years who would have the intellectual capacity to
benefit from tertiary education in Australia.

(2) Is he also able to say what proportion of Australians in
the 17 to 24 years age group are actually undergoing tertiary education compared to those in (a) the United States of America, (b) Canada, (c) Japan, (d) Switzerland, (e) Norway, (f) Denmark, (g) Sweden, (h) France and (j) the USSR.

Mr Fife-- The answer to the honourable member's questionis as follows:

(1) (a) Enrolments in Australian schools in Year 12 in 1979 were 39.6 per cent of those in Year 10 in 1977. This implies an apparent drop-out rate of 60.4 per cent between Years 10 and 12. It is an apparent, rather than true rate, because the
method of calculation does not explicitly take account of net changes to the school popUlation due to migration, nor of those students who spend more than one year in the same grade.

(b) the apparent retention rates from the first secondary year (Year 7 or Year 8) to Year 12 vary between the types of
schools specified. In 1979 the respective apparent retention rates to Year 12 were:

Government schools-28.9 per cent Catholic schools-44.1 per cent
Other Non-Government schools 87.1 per cent

Note that the above apparent retention rates are affected by transfers between types of schools, as well as by those factors mentioned in (a) above.

(c) there is variation in apparent retention rates between State systems. In 1979 the respective apparent State system retention rates to Year 12 were:

New South Wales

Victoria(a)

Queensland

South Australia

Western Australia

Tasmania(b) .

Northern Territory

.

Australian Capital Territory

% 39.9 23.1 31.1 30.8 28.4 24.8
22.7 70.5

(a) Excludes students in the Tertiary Orientation Program in Technical Schools.
(b) It is possible to obtain the Higher School Certificate either at the end of Year 11 or at the end of Year 12,
(d) of those students enrolled in Year 12 in 1973,54.5 per cent proCeeded to universities and colleges of advanced education in 1974. The corresponding proportion in relation to 1976-1977 was 48.7 per cent. It should be noted however that over this period enrolments in Technical and Further Education institutions were rising rapidly. Statistics on the proportion of Year 12 students proceeding directly on to Technical and Further Education are not available.
(e) there does appear to be a variation in urban educational participation compared to rural participation. Research studies indicate a greater retention rate to Year 12 exists in metropolitan and other urban areas than in rural areas. Once matriculation level is reached, however, the proportion which make the transition to higher education is about as high for country as for metropolitan students.
No estimates have been made of the percentage of young people aged between 17 and 24 years who would have the intellectual capacity to benefit from tertiary education in Australia.
(2) The proportion of 17 to 24 year old Australians undergoing tertiary education compared to other countries is shown in the table below. The proportions are estimates based on the latest available Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) data. Comparable data for the USSR are not available. Care should be,exercised when interpreting this data. In particular the OECD statistics for Australia and the other countries listed refer only to full time students in higher education (i.e. excluding TAFE). Other factors to be considered are differences in education systems, the emphasis placed on part time education in some of the countries listed (for example, Australia), and deficiencies in the scope and coverage of the statistics. A description of how the education systems in each country are classified is contained in 'Classification of Education Systems-Summary Volume' OECD Paris 1975.

There was an uncharacteristically bleak answer from the Minister for Education. His words were:
No estimates have been made of the percentage of young people aged between 17 and 24 years who :vould have the i~ tellectual capacity to benefit from terttary educatIOn In Australia.
In other words, we do not know the degree of intellectual wastage. We do not know where it is found and we do not propose to find out. That was an extremely depressing answer from the Minister.
Sitting suspended from 6 to 8 p.m.
Mr BARRY JONES--As I was saying before the suspensionof the sitting for dinner, on 4 December 1980 the Minister for Education gave an extremely interesting answer to a question about secondary and tertiary education. He provided some very depressing figures about the degree of wastage, that is the drop out in secondary schools from the year 7 or year 8, to year 12. There is an enormous variation in the degree of drop-outs between the three school systems. The retention rate in government schools was 28.9 per cent. In Catholic schools it was 44.1 per cent and in other non-government schools it was 87.1 per cent. In other words, more than 70 per cent of government school pupils drop out. In Catholic schools over 50 per cent drop out and in the nonGovernment schools-the so-called private schools-only 13 per cent drop out. There we see a perpetuation of the stratification of Australian society due to poverty in education.
Mr Kent-There is a class difference.
Mr BARRY JONES-There is a very strong class difference, and poverty in education is largely inherited. In the 1960s education was a very big issue in Australia. Part of the process of de-Whitlamisation since 1975 has been a turning away from education, a kind of complacent sense that we had gone as far as we could go in Australia. As I said in another debate last night, in 1980 the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development economic survey on Australia showed out that this country ranked fourteenth of 23 nations listed for educational retention rates for young people aged between 15 years and 19 years. The Williams report on Education, Training and Employment confirmed:
while 60 per cent of our 15-19 age group are in the labor force comparable figures are 24 per cent in Japan and 28 per cent in theU.S.A.
We have the paradoxical situation in Australia of the highest youth unemployment in the advanced world and also the highest youth employment. The reason that it is possible to have both figures

is that we have a very much lower participation rate at the tertiary education level. Although some attempt has been made as far as Technical and Further Education is concerned to increase the actual numbers involved, it is very far from being the equivalent of full time education.
Mr Kent-It will be worse when the Government introduces fees. Universities will be only for the rich then.
Mr BARRY JONES-If the Government introduces fees, of course, the situation will become far worse. Why have we slipped so far behind other technologically advanced nations in education? We are afflicted by a combination of ignorance, apathy and self-satisfaction-a dangerous mixture. What is the explanation for our low participation rates? I can think of three possiblities. First, Australians may be smarter than everybody else and our natural gifts do not need formal education for them to mature. The second possibility is that we are dumber than the people of comparable nations and education could not really help. Third-I suspect this is the correct answer-we are culturally so isolated and so lacking in vitality that we fail to understand that the educational revolution, aborted here, is still going on elsewhere and as a result we are slipping behind. The Williams report made it clear that there is a yawning gap between those who receive tertiary education and graduate to professional employment and those who leave school with minimal and unmarketable qualifications, with skills that are appropriate only for the contracting number of routine and repetitive jobs. The question that this nation and this Parliament have to face is: How can working class people be given access to education which maximises their skills and leads to satisfactory personal and vocational outcomes?
Mr Kent-Charge them fees.
Mr BARRY JONES-The Government's answer and the razor gang's answer is to charge them fees. The school is an excellent sorting machine. It classifies people and sets them apart. Just as cattle and sheep are branded, so school leaves its brand or caste mark for life. Few succeed in washing or shaking it off. If a person attends a poor school and lives in a poverty stricken social environment--
Mr Jacobi-He is fixed for life.
Mr BARRY JONES-He is fixed for life. The school system will do relatively little to overcome these disadvantages. If one is disadvantaged, school will tend to extend those disabilities through life. Cultural poverty seems to be inherited and it is extremely difficult to shake off.

Second Reading

Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (8.16)-Whilst this Bill was debated cognately with the States Grants (Schools Assistance) Amendment Bill I formally move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not denying the Bill a second reading, the House condemns the ill-considered and grossly inequitable changes to the funding, organisation and financing of students in tertiary education as announced in the Review of Commonwealth Functions, in that:
(l) the re-introduction of fees is not only a breach of an election undertaking but will grossly disadvantage students with limited means and will compromise Australia's research effort;
(2) the gutting of the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme will confirm tertiary education as the preserve of the rich, and
(3) the heavy handed threat in the review to 30 Colleges of Advanced Education is based on the wholly false premise that the consolidation of single purpose colleges will result in cost savings in spite of the Tertiary Education Commission's advice to the contrary'.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Hon. J. D. M. Dobie) - Is the amendment seconded?
Mr Barry Jones-I second the amendment.

Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (8.17)-Last week the people of Australia witnessed some of the most reprehensible actions yet taken by the Fraser Government. Given this Government's ccntinuing shameful record, I put to the House that that is, indeed, a mouthful. Nevertheless, I stand by it. I think the actions of last week really add to the total shame of this Government. Last week the Government cut, slashed, shaved, revised, eliminated and sold off. These were the themes of last week. Since Australia is, by any standard, one of the world's most prosperous and most affluent countries and certainly one of the major trading powers, one can only ask why. Have we gone crazy; has the manic obsession of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) with deficits and with getting his own way pushed him past the point of reason? Affluence has changed Australia, at least under this Government. We were once a country of adventurous and radical people deeply concerned with social rights. We have changed to a conservative country, more deeply concerned with preserving the standard of living of the haves, with great regard for the have-nots both in and out of Australia. Under this Government, overseas aid to developing countries, to countries in our area, the Third World countries with a shamefully low standard of living has almost disappeared. I instance the cutting off of aid to Vietnam. Surely we owe the Vietnamese people some assistance. This has not been so under this Government.

In the 1960s the people were forced into health schemes or else ran the risk of facing huge hospital bills if they were unlucky enough to become ill. Is that the way that life runs in Australia in 1981 ? Should life be a lottery for people who are too poor or whose income is so low that paying into a health insurance fund becomes a hardship? Do these people have to live life like a lottery hoping that they or their kids will not get sick?
To make matters worse the Government has slashed its overall funding of health care, forcing the States to find the necessary finance to keep their hospitals from closing. The people of New South Wales, which happens to be your State, and my State, Mr Deputy Speaker, are probably more

Health. care is just one area where ordinary people will suffer. If we look at the Lynch report we can see many more areas where suffering will occur. The Government claims. that in making the cuts that it has set out in the Lynch committee's report it has merely. trimmed off those services which were not an appropriate field of government activity. What will happen instead, though, is that huge gaps will now be created in services such as nutrition research, preventive medicine and education innovation simply because government is the only body interested in those areas. Private enterprise just will not fill those gaps. The Government pulls out of those fields because there is no profit to be made in them. Inevitably, private enterprise will not fill those gaps. The big sufferers are the public and the country as a whole.

Nevertheless, money for health care has to come from somewhere and New South Wales will have to find some $150m extra this year in order to meet its health commitments. Obviously, hospital fees will have to rise dramatically and, in fact, it was announced today that hospital bed costs will rise from $50 to $80 a day in New South Wales. This increase will be passed on to individuals in the form of greatly increased health insurance contribution rates.

The Lynch report has advocated the elimination of 17,000 Public Service jobs. The Government claims that this will be achieved largely through natural wastage. People who leave the Public Service simply will not be replaced. This is all very well. However, the people whQ leave will be those who have the brightest prospects in private enterprise. It is precisely those people who have represented the cream of the Public Service who will be most tempted to leave a service where morale is low and rewards poor to seek brighter opportunities elsewhere. This will leave a dispirited Public Service lacking in talent, overworked and unable to cope with the demands of

In the few moments left to me I would like to refer to a problem within my own electorate. In 1973 the then Whitlam Government purchased a block of land in Parrmatta at a cost of something like $5m with the intention of building a Commonwealth office building on that site. The rationale behind that was not just the housing of public servants in a building owned by the Government, by the people, but the many social benefits inherent in it. Anybody with a knowledge of town planning believes that to get the public servants away from the centre of Sydney and out into the areas where they live is a social advantage. They are saved from the problems of travelling for an hour a day and the cost of transport, and from the inherent problems of clogged roads and overutilised trains in which they travel to the city.

facilities which are very much need in my city. As

was shelved. Since then the project has

Mr JOHN BROWN-Someone mentioned the honourable member for Reid. I add also that I have noticed that the Glebe Estate is now on offer to the New South Wales Government. I hope that that Government will take over the Estate. The purchase of the Glebe Estate was one of the great achievements of the Whitlam Government. This area of old homes was bought, the houses were restored, and the traditional residents of Glebe have been able to stay there. It would be a dreadful tragedy if this land were passed over to private enterprise.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Hon. J. D. M. Dobie) -Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr McLEAN (Perth) (8.32)-1 support the statement by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) outlining the Government's Review of Commonwealth Functions. The Opposition has adopted a very curious position on the Government's expenditure reduction policy. It seems to want to have a bob each way. It spent all of the last election campaign criticising us for being a party of big government expenditure and high taxes. It was certainly curious to hear the socialists criticise the Government on those grounds. In fact, the socialist party almost won the last election by criticising the Liberal Party for being the big government, high tax party. On philosophical grounds at least, that was a curious position for Opposition members to adopt. Therefore, it is also curious that they should now criticise the Government for trying to redress the imbalance by moving towards smaller government and lower taxes-the very things they seemed to espouse during the last campaign.
The situation is even more curious when we read of the socialist party's own position regarding the public sector and taxation. Its position seems to depend on whether it is in government or opposition and whether it is approaching an election. For example, last week the Leader Opposition (Mr Hayden) severely criticised the Government for reducing the Government sector in order to allow the more productive private sector to expand. Yet in 1975, when the Leader of the Opposition was Treasurer, he said:
We expect that as the expansion of public sector activity is restrained, the opportunities for private sector expansion will improve.
When in opposition, and well removed from any election, the Leader of the Opposition said when talking about the desirability of getting lower taxes in Australia:
This sort of approach strikes directly at the democratic socialist notion that equality and equity can only be assured by a stronger public sector.
In 1978, also far removed from any election, the shadow Treasurer said that a future Labor government would:
. . . face a mammoth task in rebuilding the public sector and an equally mammoth task in convincing the electorate that it should pay a higher level of tax to enable us to do so.
It seems to me that the ALP is all over the place on this issue. It has different views at different times and seems to be guided more by votes and political expediency than by high principle. If the Labor Party is noW suggesting that we should

Review ofCommonwealth Functions

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2163

have tax cuts but no expenditure cuts then it basically is advocating its old economic policies of massive deficit blowouts, to be financed either by printing more money and causing inflation, or by going into massive debt, causing interest rate hikes and placing future generations of Australians in debt because we, the present generation, decided to live beyond our means.
The basic reason for the present cuts is to reduce the overall public sector borrowing requirement which has been a major cause of the interest rate rises in Australia. The fact remains that governments have to either tax or axe if they are to have responsible Budgets. If that is not done government expenditure rises. Any government expenditure increase that is not financed either by taxes or borrowings simply leads to more inflation. The ALP still clings to the old economic doctrines in accusing this Government of increasing unemployment to control inflation. The shadow Treasurer said this very recently when commenting on the latest consumer price index increase. The Leader of the Opposition still seems to share that belief. Yet in 1975 the Leader of the Opposition, the then Treasurer, said:
Today it is inflation itself which is the central policy problem. More inflation simply leads to more unemployment.

unemployment. It tried to explain its poor economic performance in terms of depressed international economic conditions rather than in terms of its own domestic mismanagement. At the present time, with depressed international conditions, the policies of this Government of expenditure restraint and reduced deficits have led to a situation where almost every economic indicator available shows that we in Australia now have a stronger economy than almost any other country in the industrialised world.
Employment levels over the past two years~ years of government expenditure restraint~have risen substantially. Labour force figures released this week show that total employment rose by 146,900 in the year to March 1981 and by 328,500 in the last two years, with the overall position of schoolleavers improving strongly. Certainly, unemployment is still too high, but at least we are making some progress on this very pressing problem. The Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) today issued some more figures which state the unemployment situation in April. He said:
. . . preliminary unemployment estimates released today by the Australian Bureau of Statistics were the best figures since the monthly surveys were introduced in February 1978.
The Minister further stated:

Once again, there is no consistency in ALP economic policy. It just happens to be convenient from, time to time for it to change its policies to meet the political demands of the moment. This Government has been criticised for being harsh. People have said that the razor gang decisions will cause more unemployment. I think that the Press comment in this regard has been most interesting. Many editorials have criticised the Government for not going far enough, but they have agreed that the nation would be better off for the trend established by these cuts. The Australian Financial Review last Friday stated that there will not be wholesale redundancy of public servants. It made the point that the staff reductions of about 17,000 people would be achieved by, to use its own terms, natural wastage, resignation, retirements and lower recruitment.
To suggest that decreasing government expenditure will increase unemployment ignores the fact that such expenditure is financed by taxes. Higher taxes simply mean that people have less to spend and therefore businesses have less to sell and must reduce their costs and levels of operation, including their levels of employment. The Labor Party obviously has forgotten its own experience in government, when record increases in government expenditure led to record increases in

. . . the estimated total of . . . people unemployed and looking for full time work was the lowest April figure recorded.
To suggest, therefore, that in economic terms expenditure cuts are both heartless and incorrect policy completely ignores not only economic sense but also the facts of recent economic history, both in Australia and overseas. Surely the latest employment figures and the soundness of our major economic indicators suggest not an abandoning of our policies of expenditure restraint but a strengthening of these policies as outlined in the Prime Minister's speech. I cannot see how these results indicate a harsh policy or one lacking compassion. There was no mention of hitting the needy in the welfare area, and neither should there be any mention of that. Surely this policy is the only one which offers a humane, longer term approach to our major economic and social problems: It has already led to better economic performance and to more employment. What is harsh about that? What is harsh about increasing the capacity of the Government, through these cuts, to increase its manoeuvrability by giving it the option of moving towards a combination of lower taxes and/or a concentration of more resources on the really needy sections of society? This is a flexibility which the Government did not have before these cuts were announced.

2164 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Review ofCommonwealth Functions

These cuts have proved that the capacity of the Government to move in these directions can be increased without in any way placing increased burdens on the welfare sector.
I believe the Government can do much more in this direction. For example, we should now decide not to proceed with the construction of a neW and permanent parliament house and, similarly, we should look at some of the more extravagant fringe benefits enjoyed by members of this Parliament. In this context I want to stress to the House the importance of the present public perception of members and senators of all parties. I think some cynicism and scepticism about elected representatives is a good and healthy thing but my own contact with my electorate indicates that there is a belief-rightly or wrongly, it does not matter-that we as the elected representatives confer benefits upon ourselves quickly, generously, unobtrusively and with unanimity but, at the same time, expect others in the public sector to accept restraints and cuts. I believe it is important that the cuts involve an equal bearing of the sacrifice by all sections of the public sector, including ourselves. If this program of public sector cuts, which I support~ is to have credibility and gain the degree of public acceptance we require, we should apply to ourselves the same standards we apply to others in the community.
I want to refer to the new parliament house which, at present, is expected to cost in excess of $300m at current prices. Certainly that figure will grow. Within my own party I have long been opposed to the construction of this building. I believe it is now appropriate, given the Government's report of the Review of Commonwealth Functions, to restate this view because, after all, it took some 350 decisions of the so-called razor gang to save something like $560m of taxpayers' funds. With one decision not to proceed with the new parliament house we could effect savings equal to 40 per cent of that total amount achieved by the razor gang. When the decision to proceed with the new parliament house was made it was said that it would provide the nation with a sense of national identity, a sense of national pride, that it would be a memorial to parliamentary democracy, and so forth. Very lavish phrases and many superlatives were thrown about. I reject that proposition on several grounds. Firstly, the integrity of parliamentary democracy or the achieving of a sense of national pride does not depend on bricks and mortar-it depends on what happens inside that building; it depends on the quality of government and the dignity of the members of parliament who sit within that building.

The second point I make relates to some of the other major projects in the Australian Capital Territory which are being shelved at the moment, such as the national museum. The point is that most of my constituents in Perth, and most residents of Western Australia, because of our isolation and the present structure of air fares in this country, are denied the opportunity to visit and see these so-called national buildings which their taxes have helped to build. I sometimes think that some of these things, such as the National Art Gallery and national museums, ought to be built in some of the more populous centres of this country.
The third point I make in regard to the new parliament house is that the building we occupy now is an historic building and with some minor additions, and some changes to our present Standing Orders, it could certainly suit our foreseeable needs. As I said earlier, I extend my argument in this regard to include also certain fringe benefits enjoyed by members of parliament, particularly those benefits which have no real influence on how well we do our jobs. I mention the need to restrict members' fringe benefits not because they constitute major public expenditure savings; they do not. It is not a matter of dollars and cents. I raise the matter because some action ought to be taken in those areas if we, as members of parliament, wish to apply expenditure restraints in other areas of the public sector and in so doing maintain credibility and some degree of public acceptance for these expenditure cuts.
I support the concept embraced in the statement by the Prime Minister last Thursday. It is in accord with every major strand of economic thinking by respectable bodies overseas. Within Australia there have been many comments in support of this strategy. I would like to quote from the latest monthly publication of ANZ Bank Business Indicators. That publication said:
The outstanding feature of the Australian economy is the lift now occurring in private sector demand and in total employment.
It goes on to explain rises in consumption expenditure and the massive increases in private investment expenditure in Australia, and then states:
This expansion by the private sector should lift the flow of revenue to Government. At the same time it should also obviate the need for direct Government involvement in stimulating economic activity . . . In particular, the growth in private sector economic activity should provide scope for some reduction in the growth of Government spending without adversely affecting total economic activity. Considering present and expected growth in total investment expenditure, it would be desirable for the Government to lessen its demands in order to maintain an economic environment conducive to further abatement in the rate ofinflation.

Review o/Commonwealth Functions

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2165

On economic grounds, responsible management grounds and compassionate grounds I believe the decisions of this committee of review announced last Thursday are responsible and in the best long term interests of this country.

Mr MOUNTFORD (Banks) (8.47)-This debate is in response to the statement of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to Parliament last Thursday on the Review of Commonwealth Functions. We are told that this review had as its aims the streamlining and fining down of Commonwealth operations, partly by withdrawing from functions more appropriately handled by the States or private enterprise and partly by rationalising the functions which properly belong to the Commonwealth. The Prime Minister went on further to say that the aim of the review in the wider perspective was to establish the conditions under which there can be increased opportunities for all and better care for those in need. To complete the scenario of the results of the review's findings, one needs to read to the end of page 3 of
the statement.

If one did not know our Prime Minister and those who support him, or at least sit behind him, one would think that those who sit on the Government benches really had the interests of all Australians at heart. But we know differently. What has been written in this review is fairy story stuff and is unworthy of being espoused by this Government. T~e speech writers who prepared the scenario should be writing for Disneyland or the Hanna-Barbera organisation, because they are writing about matters affecting Australians of which their political masters could not care less.

ceptance of a large Public Service and a protective

role for the state, democracy commits itself to an

irreversible descent into totalitarianism and com-

munism. Professor Friedman is firm about this

prospect, and has stated:

If we continue our present trend and our free society is replaced by a col1ective society, the intel1ectuals who have done so much to drive us down this path will not be the ones who run the society; the prison, the insane asylum, or the graveyard would be their fate . . . or shaH we have the wisdom and the courage to change our course, to learn from experience, and to benefit from a rebirth of freedom.
Obviously Government members have been made disciples of Milton Friedman's theories because what they are proposing has a marked similarity. The Government had best beware as there are grave disadvantages in being associated with a policy that will not work. It should be noted that this arch exponent of monetarism, Professor Friedman, has already been forced to disassociate himself from Israel and Chile where he was once celebrated as a prophet. The Friedman policy in Britain has so far produced only high inflation, deep internal stagnation, the highest unemployment since the Great Depression and some indication that he would like to be detached from these policies as well. I believe the Government is misreading both the economic signs and the political signs in taking the actions outlined in the review and by reducing the public sector community involvement. The policies of smaller government espoused by the Thatcher and Reagan administration have not worked and will not work.
The attack which we see on the public sector at the present time is a generalised attack. Education, urban services and other conventional functions of government as well as assistance to the sick, public housing funding, assistance to the unemployed and the unemployable and other government enterprises are all under attack. We are told that people must be left free to choose. The divesting of government activities to private enterprise is supposed to create a greater freedom of choice, more employment, economic growth and a better life for all. In putting forward such theories, of course, the Government is pretending that the giant financial institutions and multinational corporations do not exist. These corporations control and govern the world economynot governments. These corporations have the power to set prices and to create employment or unemployment, whichever suits their need, from country to country. They influence extensively the taste of consumers and, similarly, they organise their supplies of raw materials. With all of this influence these corporations erode the power of the market.
Does anyone suggest that corporations such as the Bank of America, Shell, IBM, Philips, Exxon and Esso do not have a great influence on the finances and market forces of Australia? It is because of these influences that the Government's

2166 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Review of Commonwealth Functions

policies will not work. The disbandment of the Prices Justification Tribunal will make it even more difficult to control any excesses. It needs to be remembered that the abolition of the Prices Justification Tribunal will not be welcomed by the rural industry also. Inquiries into stevedoring, woolbroking charges, spare parts and fuel prices all save primary producers a lot of money. The National Farmers Federation recently said that PJT recommendations meant a $43.5m saving on fuel prices for primary producers alone.
I will now turn my attention to some of the more specific matters outlined in the Review of Commonwealth Functions. One of the hardest hit areas is that of education. In that area we have seen monetary reductions over a wide range of activities. The first item is to restrict the opportunity for persons over 19 years of age to receive a Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowance while doing their Higher School Certificate through the Adult Secondary Education Assistance Scheme. This is a miserly decision as not more than 2,000 persons throughout Australia took advantage of the scheme during 1980. Many of the people who were unable to complete their HSC during their school life saw this as the only opportunity to better themselves in later life. Similarly, the decision to introduce fees for students undertaking second degree or diploma courses in universities and colleges of advanced education is obviously only the thin edge of the wedge for the eventual reintroduction of fees for all1evels of courses.
Over a period of many years this country has established a proud record in the availability of free education which has enabled all persons, including young people, irrespective of their financial background, the opportunity of education so that they can procure a better standard of living for themselves and their families. Education must never be linked to the financial standing of individuals. The restriction of payment of TEAS allowances to those persons required to live away from home by reason of geography or home conditions means that the only students eligible will be those from country locations. At the present time the scheme is a -joke as the majority of students receiving the allowance come from country locations. Because of the fact that their families usually operate under income splitting schemes this allows them to be satisfactorily means tested so that they receive the benefit.
The supposed rationalisation of colleges of advanced education will do nothing but reduce the effectiveness of these colleges. In New South Wales those hit the hardest are those located in the heavily populated areas where the need is

greatest. The Milperra College of Advanced Education, which is within my electorate, is one of those affected. The population of the region served by this college is as large as that of South Australia. It is the only college in the southwestern region of Sydney-the fastest growing metropolitan area in any part of Australia. The college has 800 students, 62 on the academic staff and 62 non-academic staff. The college conducts a great many courses including those concerned with teacher education, multicultural education, career education, social welfare and the arts. Should that college be amalgamated, it would deprive access to tertiary education to 20 per cent of those students because of travel problems. It may be of interest to note that few of the colleges of advanced education in the country were affected. For instance, the Northern Rivers College of Advanced Education, which is in the electorate of the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony), has not been the subject of any cuts of the razor gang. Tf one has seen this college one would say that it should be the first to be affected. So much for rationale and fairness!
Another area to suffer from the Government's decision is Aboriginal affairs. The cruel reductions by the Government have seen the percentage of the national Budget spent on the recovery of Aboriginal Australians reduced from 0.9 per cent in 1974-75 to 0.6 per cent in 19S0-SJ-a reduction of over 30 per cent in the six years of this Fraser Government. The hopes concerning the lives of these, our fellow Australians, have been dashed once again-just after their hopes had been raised in the early 1970s by a caring and sympathetic Labor Government. It makes the policy of self determination impossible because they are being denied the resources necessary for management. This is not good enough for the Aboriginal men, women and children who should be treated as equal partners in the development of our great country.
The 44 pages of the Prime Minister's statement made no commitment to the future but instead showed that the Government had opted out of significant areas of government responsibility. It has no mandate from the people for doing so. I suggest that the Australian people will not support the supposed free enterprise government for much longer because they will come to realise between now and the next election that this Government has abrogated its responsibilities to others rather than pursuing a course of providing incentives and assistance to those in our coummunity who are most in need of them. In those important areas of education, health, job-creation, pensioner

Government has failed the people of Australia.

The Government's cynical exercise of cutting back on necessary welfare areas so that it can give some rebates in direct taxation payments in 1982-83-just prior to the next election-whilst increasing indirect taxes, will not be accepted by the Australian electorate because this Government has lost its credibility. The big difference between members sitting on this side of the House and those on the Government side is that we care about people. We care about their quality of life, the type of world that they live in and the type of world that their children will grow up in. We be-
lieve the benefits derived from the resources of this great country should be shared amongst the people of Australia. We have always held to those principles in the past and will hold to them in the future because we belong to a party of principle. We believe only governments can ensure that people receive their rightful share of a country's
wealth and that they are cared for in time of need. We do not believe the private sector can ensure
those things.

The report of the Review of Commonwealth Functions reveals some strange bedfellows. I do not think the, report is a sound one. There are to be cuts in government expenditure but I doubt whether many wool growers.,-certainly the' representatives of'tnc' wool growers-will consider that the Government is taking a step forward by getting rid of the Australian Wool Testing Authority. When the Authority was run by private enterprise it was inefficient and costly and did not do the job expected of it by the wool growers. Under its existing management it is efficient and profitable. What are his economic circumstances, especially if he is a low income earner living from ,hand to mouth, not a pensioner but a low income earner with a family?
Mr Hyde-You can help the poor people. .
'Mr ,SCHOLES-I am not talking abput the poor people either; I am talking about the ordinary people who live in the conununity, the Australians whom some honou,rable members opposite ought to consider sometimes. What does the Government say? If that family is unlucky enough and .cares enough to want to do something abQut that child then the Government says that the user should pay! The ,misfortune of ill health is not s~mething that we choose ourselves. It is not an afHiction, and it is certainly not a sign of some evil thing that we have done to ourselves or our children. It is something that hap~ pens to us. The user pays-wha~ utter rot! The user pays idea is an easy way out for p~ople who want to avoid their responsibilities to their fellow human beings. fellow human beings. It has no' heart, no care, and no responsibility.
It is all right for the Prime Minister, he talked today about unnecessary subsidies. Ask him to come into this Parliament and list the subsidies he gets on his own property. In many instances they are greater than the costs for the sick. But the user pays! In the field of health the community has a responsibility to itself t~ ensure that those people who have need of treatment are entitled to get that treatment at the most reasonable cost possible. To criticise the situation of payment for health services on the basis of ability to pay is to talk about abandoning the sick because their ability to pay is the lowest of any section of the community. The previous speaker, the honourable member for Wilmot, made it a virtue that the poor should not get treatment because they cannot pay. Within 12 months it will cost $700 to $900 a year to be adequately covered by a scheme. That is the cost that the Government is about to infiiqt on the average family in Australia. Not too many families, large families especially, 'will be able to 'afford the cost. When the Government inflicts that charge onthose families it whould just remember that many will not be able to afford it. They will not be adequately covered.
What is a public hospital to do when the father of that family has a massive coronary, arrives at the hospital door, does not have adequate medical cover and does not meet the criteria for being disadvantaged? Does it say to him when he needs open ,heart surgery and intensive care treatment: 'lam sorry. Take him home and arrange for the cheapest funeral' you can afford'? The medical costs involved to save the father, the breadwinner, of such a fiunily are between $10,000 and $20,000. The user pays! Perhaps we should say that the family should sell the house, as people do in America, the free enterprise way. Not on your life.-Most Australians disagree with the Governmerit's proposals on health. It is a coward's castle. <;learly, the Government is running away from its responsibilities as a national governme~t.

The Defence Printing Establishment carries out at a high technological level a considerable amount of sensitive and important printing, which is necessary for the armed forces of this country. It is to be sold off so that someone can make a quick buck. Damn the armed forces and damn the requirements they might have for accurate and secure printing! The Government has no worries at all about the people who work there.
The Government must have a friend in Melbourne who is prepared to make a big donation and who wants quickly a printing works or some section of a printing works. For any government to sell off any essential defence establishment as a cost saving exercise is a repudiation of its responsibilities for national security and defence preparedness; and that is exactly what is happening in this instance. The Defence Printing Establishment provides a highly confidential printing service for the Department of Defence. The Government is saying that its printing requirements should go out to private contractors. Honourable members ought to read what the Prime Minister said in the Review of Commonwealth Functions because the Defence work is not being transferred to the Government Printer. The Government Printer's functions are being sold to private enterprise, and that is' all there is to it.
There is only one thing that can be said with regard to Canberra. Canberra is completely defenceless in the face of a ruthless and exploitive Federal Government. It is about time the Government gave the people of Canberra, whether they want it or not, the responsibility of making their own decisions with regard to their own money. The Government talks about letting people make decisions on how to spend their money yet in Canberra it has a principality with an absentee prince and an authority which it is paying but which has no decision-making power at all. It is a disgrace to any democratic society and it is a disgrace to this Parliament that that situation continues.
Mr HYDE (Moore) (9.31)-The observation I must make to the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) is that a loud voice and assertion is no substitute for argument. If the Opposition is going to insist that all these things which the Review of Commonwealth Functions mentions can be retained in the public sector and that they will necessarily be more efficient and so on and so forth, I point out that it is as incumbent on the Opposition to provide argument as it is on us. The report of the co-called razor gang is something of a watershed. To understand that we need to understand some recent history. Ever since 1975 the Liberal Party has talked of small government. In 1980 we introduced what was the biggest

Budget in real terms in Australia's history. That was followed by an election which the Australian Labor Party came close to winning, although it was still appreciably short of the majority of public opinion; but it achieved that result by costing the Liberal Party's promises. Since that time, in terms of small government, the rhetoric from this side of the House has been excellent. The Governor-General's Speech was clearly a speech in favour of small government. On behalf of the Government the Governor-General said:
My Government will give particular attention to encouraging the growth of the private sector and to increasing the freedom of choice of taxpayers in spending their own incomes. It will be an important policy objective to continue to restrain public expenditure so as to provide scope for reducing the burden oftaxation.
We read a similar recommendation in the speech of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to the South Australian Liberal Party. I will not read that speech as honourable members can look it up. The Tuesday before last in this House the Prime Minister said:
The Government's direction has always been that individ-
uals are the best judges of how to spend their own earnings,
that the disadvantaged and the needy must be provided for, that the tax burden must be reduced, that employment must be increased, that freedom ofchoice must be paramount.
I agree with all those things, and I agree with them very strongly indeed. Small government requires two things: It requires a small public sector and it requires small regulations of the private sector. The two areas we have to get out of are these: The public controlling of the society, because we own it and take all the decisions; and we have to get out of the business of telling all the individuals in the society how they will spend their money and, as a result, how they will occupy their time. Last Thursday'S statement was a very important first step in the right direction.

The next group of critics are those who assert that the Government will not carry the cuts through. They say that the proposals are real and important but the Government will not have the courage to see them through. Only time will tell that. I expect that there will be some backsliding on some of the items. I do not think that it is reasonable for any group of men to have picked all those items accurately to the point that they have foreseen every difficulty. I accept that; that will not worry me. What will worry me is if another list is not presented at Budget time, that the process is not ongoing, that we cannot look to more cuts in more significant areas. It will not worry me if a few of the items fall by the wayside because of unforseen circumstances.
The last group of critics suggest that the cuts are too small. I certainly think that the cuts are too small. We have further to go. But bear in mind that this is a very significant turn-around in direction. For the first time. I can say that government is not getting big more slowly, but is actually getting smaller. That makes the cuts very important indeed. Where do we go from here? I suggest that we look at introducing university fees for people wanting to obtain first degrees. For the life of me, I do not understand why people who come from relatively low income areas, such as Lockridge in my electorate, should pay for the education of people who, almost entirely, come from very high income families. That does not seem to be justice to me. Why should not those who have access to an education that allows them to earn much higher lifetime incomes pay for that education so that it is not a burden on those who earn low lifetime incomes. They could obtain money for the fees through a student loans scheme and pay it back over a lifetime in amounts geared to their incomes. This does not have to be a hardship for anybody. It will allow the poor people to enter the system.
The isolated patients travel accommodation assistance scheme is impossible to administer fairly because of its complexities. It is too complicated for the Government to administer. The new Parliament House and the proposed Defence Force Academy should also be cut from Government expenditure. We could make this present Parliament House big enough by changing the Standing Orders so that we did not have divisions by ambush. We could then use West Block, the Hotel Kurrajong and the old Hotel Canberra fully as Westminster uses all of Whitehall. We could get over the problems of this building for the cost of

amendment the Standing Orders. Recommendations on decentralisation assistance grants involve $9.6m. As far as I can make out the people who are supposed to benefit most from the multicultural broadcasting do not want it. About $21.2m of the taxpayers' money is involved. In relation to airport development, the business of putting up airports seems to be an election hazard. Airports have taken over from dams. Now during elections we feel airports coming on. Railways are a special problem. Australian National Railways is expected to have an operation loss of $53m on a revenue expenditure of $170m. The liquefied petroleum gas subsidy involves $25m and on the petroleum products freight subsidy, $125m. Those subsidies fly right in the face of the parity pricing policy. There is plenty of room left to go further and we should be contemplating further cuts now.

The rhetoric of small government or less government intervention is only a cover-up for an attack by the Fraser Government on the living standard of the Australian people. Behind the Government's rhetoric there is an assault on all the civilian services of modern government. One does not have to look far to establish where the orders for such an assault are emanating from. I will quote a few lines from the March bulletin of the Australian Industries Development Association. The bulletin states:

of cuts, ceiling and restraints indicates a deliberate onslaught of the conservative forces on the living standard of the Australian family.
Why was it, that at this point of our history, the capitalist conservative establishment chose to abandon its human face and use the ugly, mean face of the Prime Minister instead? In the era of the 1950s and 1960s, when the post-war capitalist economy was booming, there were few dissenting voices in the Western World to the demand that governments intervene in the management of the economy so that unemployment and inflation

The political difficulty of biting deeply into welfare expenditures is acknowledged. Nevertheless it is clear that not only constraint on growth but expenditure reduction is necessary in the welfare field.

were kept to the minimum. There was a general consensus that only governments could provide services such as low-cost housing, health care for the disadvantaged and urban transportation,

Further it stated:

which by their nature are not available from the

2176 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Review afCommonwealth Functions

private sector or on which the private economy defaults as there are no profits to be made in these fields. It was generally accepted that measures to assist the unemployed, to extend welfare payments, to increase old age pensions, to introduce environmental protection, consumer protection and so on were the proper function of government. The role of government was to protect the individual in our society. In other words, government was used to hone off the rough and harsh edges of the capitalist system. Hence the introduction of Medibank, migrant translator services, increased funds for schools, colleges and universities, abolition of tertiary fees and a score of other measures to improve the quality of life in the early 1970s in Australia. Unfortunately, the introduction of better welfare services in Australia was delayed because of the reactionary Liberal Government under Menzies. By the time we started to introduce mild social reforms the capitalist economy was already heading towards a deep crisis from which it has not emerged even today.
If we have this analysis in mind it will be much easier to understand the sudden switch to Fraserism. Whilst the economy of the Western capitalist countries, including ours, enjoyed a period of steady growth and relatively stable prices in the 1960s, there were already forces in operation which later upset the economic equilibrium and drastically altered both the economic and the political context of macro-economic policy. The phenomenon I refer to is the continual growing of corporate concentration, an unavoidable feature of capitalist development. The enormous size and power of multinational. corporations enabled them to resist so-called free market forces. They could dictate prices and increase them at will, thus avoiding the discipline of the market. They effectively used their power to set aside the market and increase prices and profits. The ability of multinational corporations to use their power to this effect became a new intractable cause of inflation. Prices now rise not as a consequence of strong demand but because of the strong upward push of corporations.
Of course, we do not hear the Treasurer (Mr Howard) or the Prime Minister talk about the upward price push of large multinational corporations which control the market forces. They still fallaciously talk about the upward push of wages knowing only too well that wage increases do not precede but follow price rises affected by the power of big corporations. This new development-inflation caused by concentration of corporate power and not by increased demand-made itimpossible for the Government

to use the old tried-out capitalist weapon of resorting to create a pool of unemployed to reduce inflation. The Prime Minister and the Treasurer resorted to such tactics; they created more than half a million unemployed. To their consternation their inhuman strategy did not work; nor is it working today.
The only result of Fraser's economic policies was the lowering of living standards of Australian families and as a consequence the Prime Minister came close to losing last October's elecion. The last mini-Budget, the abandonment of the Govermnent's responsibilities for our health care and hospitals, the withdrawal of half tax indexation, the razor gang's proposals for reduction in employment opportunities and the sell-out of the people's assets will further erode the living standards of the people.
The razor gang's proposals will not benefit any section of our community bar a few speculators who consort with this Government. In the main, the razor gang's activities were motivated by the dogmatic ideological views of the Prime Minister. Take for instance the sell-out of government assets and land. What kind of business accumen do those people opposite who support such a proposal have? Did I hear the Treasurer say the other day in the House that he and his colleagues are advised by people who have extensive business experience in the private sector? What kind of business prudence is the sell-out of one's assets to pay the bill, or as it is in this case, to balance the Budget?
The honourable gentlemen opposite proved that they are very good businessmen when they manage their own affairs. They are all hanging on to their assets and accumulating even more. Some people were so eager to lay their hands on more cash or land that they got in trouble. Yet when it comes to the people's assets the Government wants to squander them. This is what happens when the custodians of publicly owned land are people such as the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch). It is like putting Dracula in charge of the blood bank. The people of Australia will remember how the Liberal National Country Party Government sold their assets and how they squandered them. There is nothiflg that can save this reactionary lot in the next election. We will have a Labor government in 1983.
