Review ofCommonwealth Functions

have throughout the administration of government services is an understanding of the benefits that can result from efficient administration and a commitment to that which is well deserving and well delivered. A knowledge and understanding of good administration can provide many more services and provide them more cheaply. Over a period of five or six years or even longer-infact since the Labor Party came into governmentthere have been many examinations of the functions of government and the administration of government. It is only this Government that has taken decisions.
Honourable members opposite will remember ~he Royal Commission into Australian Government Administration on which there is no action whatsoever. Honourable members on this side of the House will remember the Administrative Review Committee set up in 1975 under the chairmanship of Sir Harry Bland. There have been investigations of all sorts. They include the Medibank review of 1976, the review of delivery of services financed by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs, the inquiry into care of the aged and infirm, the Myers inquiry into unemployment benefit, the Norgard inquiry into the Commonwealth Employment Service and the Baily task force inquiring into health and welfare and review of procedures and processes in the Department of Social Security. A great deal of examination and thought has gone into a careful assessment of government administration. Each step of the way this Government is seeking to improve its efficiency and ability to give services that are well thought out and based on sound policies and to deliver them in an efficient way.
The House of Representatives Standing Committee on Expenditure is another example of what this Government has sought to do to ensure effective and economic implementation of government policy and administration. The Auditor-General is charged with reporting on the efficiency with which departments are administered and the way in which programs are delivered. I believe that that record alone, without the decisions made by the Review of Commonwealth Functions committee, demonstrates that the anti-socialist parties have a commitment to effective accountability and responsibility in government and a desire to ensure that programs do not go on year after year. Established programs can become ingrained so that at Budget time departments put up their bids to extend and increase their every section, every function and every division of administration. It is time there was a reassessment, a re-examination of exactly what departments and people do. It is ridiculous that the

Australian Capital Territory has had a Collector of Scalps for so many years. I understand that the other place in this Parliament has had people coming in for five years to collect the mail on Saturday mornings even though there have not been mail deliveries. I find that typical of examples that I see from day to day of things which should be attended to. Let us start right here, in this place, and extend it so that there is a proper understanding of responsibility, of effective management,and an effective corporate view.
The findings of the Bland committee indicated that, despite close questioning, of the 26 heads of Government departments only two on a regular basis sought to find out how their department was standing, with a running sheet, with a cash flow situation, to see whether they were operating effectively and within budget. It is unbelievable that only two heads of Government departments took the trouble to find out whether they were living within their budgets. I think that a sense of corporate planning and corporate responsibility is needed with the Australian Public Service. I hope that the next step that this Government takes will be to ensure that incentives and also restrictions are placed on the Public Service so that attitudes can be fostered and so that, without cuts or reductions, services can be delivered efficiently and cheaply.
The sorts of proposals I believe would bring this about include the requirement that all Government departments live within their budgets unless directed by Cabinet to undertake additional expenses. I do not believe any government department has a right to overrun its budget through poor management, unless it is directed to take up additional programs. I do not believe that any department has a right to draw down the Advance to the Minister for Finance unless a proper submission has been approved by Cabinet. I do not believe that that process is proper. The processes of poor budgeting, poor accountability and poor assessment cannot be tolerated. The easy way out is to make those poor calculations and then draw down additional finances by way of the Advance to the Minister for Finance. Any department which underspends its budget without detriment to its projects or programs and can carry forward those funds to the next years should do so. There must be encouragement for efficiency. There must be some sort of fostering of that which is excellent in administration. Too often in this country, too often in the departments of the Commonwealth Government, and always in the departments of State governments, those people who overspend, who are inefficient and who ask for more are the

I wish to comment on some of the extraordinary events of the last few days which have been generated by this Government that espouses small government. We have seen attacks on specific purpose grants for health and housing, and the extraordinary interim formula which has been put forward to bridge the gap between this financial year, with the demise of the 39.87 per cent formula, and next year, when we will see general purpose grants to the States based on a percentage of total income tax. We have seen the extraordinary way in which the States have been cheated out of $70m and how they have been locked into a continuing loss because that $70m will be part of the new formula, as a percentage of total taxes as it applies year after year after year. 2180 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Review ofCommonwealth Functions

There is an argument for restricting Loan Council borrowings.According to the Prime Minister's special program, they have already been endorsed to an extent of $5,000m. Now the States are after another $5,000m. It may be that they are asking too much in that area. When this Government talks of control it does not mean the control of finance to provide the infrastructure for resource development to aid transnational companies which seek to exploit our nonrenewable national resources; it means the control of expenditure on items which satisfy basic social needs such as health, public housing, public transport and so on. Let us see when the Premiers meet again in June whether it is strong enough to rein in infrastructure expenditure for the servicing of resource development projects.
Let us look at what the Government intends to do in the area of environment and home affairs. Firstly, it says that responsibility for regulation and control of nuclear activities in Australia is to be implemented as far as possible by the States. It wants to hand over responsibility for the financing of the Northern Territory environmental regulatory services to the Northern Territory Government. It wants to hand over the day to day management of our national parks in the Northern Territory and the Northern Territory Government. If there is one truly national legislative responsibility it is in relation to the dangerous, dirty and selfish uranium and nuclear industry. The Government's proposal to hand over to the States the regulation of this industry is grossly irresponsible. It will create more government, not less government. It will create greater costs, not lower costs, and more risks to the people who will be working in the industry.
There is no uniform effective environmental assessment legislation to process conventional resource developments, let alone uranium developments. The situation is that resource development legislation,. under which the States are supposed to assess the environmental aspects of such development, is lacking in uniformity. Only the Commonwealth, New South Wales and Victoria have legislation which allows the public display of environmental impact statements and/or public, local or regional inquiries. Even then the Federal Environment Protection (Impact of Proposals) Act relies on other constitutional powers, such as the issue of export licences and Loan Council Authority for borrowings to implement recommendations of these inquiries when they are held. In Queensland and Western Australia, the two major resource development States, there is

no worthwhile conventional environmental legislation. There is no provision for public display of environmental impact statements and no provision for public inquiries. It is the action Minister who instigates a resource development proposal, who makes the final decision on whether it will go ahead. This position is bad enough, but the current legislative position under which uranium projects are assessed and the industry operates is even worse. It will become worse under this Government.
There is a multiplicity of current Federal and State Acts which govern the development of the uranium Industry. On a Federal level there is the Atomic Energy Act, the Environmental Protection (Alligator Rivers Region) Act and the Environmental Protection (Nuclear Codes) Act. The Northern Territory Acts include the Uranium Mining (Environment Control) Act and the Radioactive Ores and Concentrates (Packing and Transport) Act. In South Australia there is the Mining Act and the Mining Act Amendment Act (No.2). In Queensland there is the Mining Act and the Radioactive Substances Act. In Western Australia there is the Mining Act and the Nuclear Activities Regulation Act. There is a mUltiplicity of almost meaningless legislation which has no overall Federal umbrella to pull the whole thing together and give it support. Of course, as an Opposition we do not support it. Now the Government intends to make the situation even worse. It intends to hand over responsibility for regulation and control of the uranium and nuclear industry to the States.
Against that background let us look at the multiplicity of projects which the Government is talking about. There is the Ben Lomond project in Queensland and the Honeymoon proposal in South Australia which was endorsed yesterday by the Tonkin Liberal Government. This provides for in situ acid leaching of uranium from the ore body and the return of the radioactive liquid waste back into the water-table. That is the sort of mine that is proceeding at this stage under State legislation. The Government wants to give the States even more power. There is also the Beverley project, the Koongarra project, the Yeelirrie project and the Roxby Downs project. All in all that constitutes about $1.5 billion worth of development in uranium mining. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table which shows the multiplicity of these reserves, the ore grades and ownership which is, of course, foreign dominated.

Mr WEST-I thank the 'House. All of these projects will lead to a tremendous overproduction. Even the Australian Atomic Energy Commission, that wonderful environmental authority, forecasts that by 1985 world demand will be about 39,000 tonnes. Our share of the world market, the Commission says, will be 8,000 tonnes maximum. This Government is pushing the States into a position where they will be producing 14,000 tonnes by the middle and late 1980s. We will never be able to sell the stuff and there will be an unseemly scramble to sell on a declining wOJ:ld market with a resulting weakening safeguards and fall in in environmental standards.
In this situation, giving nuclear ,regulatory powers to the States is the worst thing a government could possibly do. It will lead to competition in mines. It will lead to plans for enrichment plants. It will eventually increase demand for nuclear power stations. The Premiers of Western Australia, South Australia, Victoria, and Queensland and the Chief Minister of the Northern Territory almost slaver at the mouth in anticipation of completing the nuclear power cycle by the establishment of enrichment plants and power stations. They are all on the public record as being in support of completing the development of the nuclear cycle. In the uranium and nuclear areas we can expect to see the same sort of competition which now bedevils the nation in the area of coal

development. An amount Of $9 billion is involved in coal development; $8 billion in aluminium smelters and $10 billion in power stations. No wonder the Government is worried about the Loan Council. The same situation applies in relation to coal to oil plants. Competition is occurring throughout the States. Now the Government actually intends to encourage competition to extend into the uranium and nuclear industry by handing over coptrol to the States. The Government does not even know how it is going to legislate for this.
Mr Uren-And cut back the funds for social infrastructure.
Mr WEST-It will be doing that at the same time. Major uranium producing nations-even the United States of America and Canada-all have federal legislative pontrol over nuclear activity. We would be the only banana republic in theworlq that had the stupidity to say to the States: 'You look after this dangerous, dirty and sen!!itive industry'. In the United States regulation of all activities related to uranium mining processing and nuclear power generation is under the control of the Federal Government. There is no question about that. Control over all radioactive materials rests with the United States Nuclear Regulatory Committee. What does the Government think about that with its small government

Mr KENT (Hotham) (10.45)-It is my intention to speak tonight on the decision of the Tasmanian Government to proclaim the Franklin and Lower Gordon rivers, in Tasmania's south-west wilderness, as part of a national park. the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) will join me in those congratulations because only recently, on 8 April, he said in reply to a question by the honourble member for Cunningham (Mr West):
The south-western wilderness area is land of great beauty. It is unique; it is irreplaceable. I hope that it can be listed in the World Heritage list.
Unlike the honourable member for Franklin, on this point I agree with the Prime Minister. After touring south-west Tasmania recently in a light plane, I believe that the flooding of rivers in the area for hydro-electric power would have been an ecological disaster. This magnificent, rugged wilderness is one of the few remaining untouched areas in Australia and, if left in its present state, will provide a unique heritage for generations of Australians to come. The south-west has the reputation of being one of the finest bush walking areas of the world. Its topography is varied. It has magnificent examples of glacial landforms, forested valleys and rivers that have formed enormous gorges and its coastline is extremely rugged. It presents enormous scope for geological and archaeological research by scientists. Only recently, in January and February, it was discovered that Aboriginals had inhabited the region during the last Ice Age. These archaeological discoveries were directly threatened by the plans of the Hydro-Electric Commission of Tasmania to flood the area. The invaluable heritage of the relics found there, and those yet to be found, would have been lost forever. There would be no going back, or second chances, if the decision to flood these areas had gone ahead.
Let us examine why the Tasmanian Hydro Electric Commission would commit such environmental vandalism. The destruction of the area was designed to yield 180 megawatts of power. I invite honourable members to compare this with the output of Loy Yang, which is 4,000 megawatts, or even the Newport power station, which is 500 megawatts. This alone is indicative of what a thoughtless plan the flooding of the Franklin was. In this plan the Hydro-Eelectric Commission was abetted and supported by the Upper House of Tasmania. I understand that that House is not exactly a democratically elected body; that it perpetuates itself by a gerrymander. Lest honourable members should think that I am alone in my opinion on this matter, I propose to quote from an editorial in the Melbourne Age of 5 May. The editorial states:
The question is no longer simply the fate of south-west Tasmania. The Council-and to some extent the HEC-have made It a fight about who is running the State. A small group of conservative independents, elected on one of Australia's worst gerrymanders--
Mr Hodgman-Oh, that's terrible.

Mr KENT-I am quoting not from a socialist newspaper but from the Melbourne Age. The editorial continues:
is endeavouring to block the will of a democratically elected government on a major issue-and promising to do so indefinitely. . . . last Thursday's decision by the Government to declare the gorges of the Franklin and Olga a national park is a welcome sign of resolve.
If the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) wants to hear some more I will quote the editorial further:
Mr Lowe should now keep up the pressure by legislation to abolish the Council gerrymander. The Fraser Government should help by persuading its Tasmanian supporters to respect both constitutional convention and the landscape of the south-west.
May I hope that for once the Federal Government will stop playing politics, and will come to the assistance of the Tasmanian Government and help to preserve the south-west wilderness.
Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (10.50)-1 know it must be of great comfort to all the Tasmanian members to know that the best interests of their State are now safely in the hands of the honourable member for Hotham (Mr Kent). Last week some members of this House attended the keynote address at the Australian Mining Industry Council Minerals Outlook Seminar given by its Senior Vice-President, Mr H. M. Morgan. As not many Labor members actually attended that address so that they would really find out something about the mining industry-apart from the honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating), who has a degree of responsibility in this area which is not shared by many of his colleagues-I believe that I should point out to the House some of the realities about the mining industry. The Australian Labor Party appears to think the industry is some sort of bottomless honeypot from which it can get goodies non-stop to pay for its welfare programs, which in fact, are not generally aimed at people in need but at people from whom it hopes to buy a vote.
The points made by Mr Morgan are worth stating to the House. He said:
The current Federal/State rivalry over who gets the biggest slice of the government share of mineral related revenue threatens to become the single most important, and divisive, issue in Australian politics during the next five years. And the mining industry faces the chilling prospect of being caught in the crossfire. At the present time both sides in this Federal/ State row argue that they have the right to impose taxes on the industry because the resources are owned by the people they represent. I doubt that the members of the pre-Federation Constitutional Conventions who reserved Crown ownership of minerals to the States ever thought that position would be challenged.
I think it is worth pointing out too that Mr Morgan said:

2188 REPRESENTATIVES 7 May 1981

Adjournment

While the current Federal Government has repeatedly rejected the proposition that it impose a resources tax on the industry, continual urgings for it to do so have resulted in a number of States engaging in reviews of their own royalty charges. Quite naturally, they are not prepared to run the risk of allowing another authority to benefit from the development of their resources. Equally, the mining industry looks askance as the prospect of it being subjected to increasingly heavier charges designed to satisfy the phony political objective of ensuring that the Australian community receives an equitable share of mineral development proceeds. The community's share in the industry is already higher than that of any other industry. Last year, for example, the government share in the mining and exploration sector of the industry was 55.8 per cent of the pre-tax operating profit of the industry. How much more does this industry have to pay to satisfy the political objectives of those who cannot understand that the total government share in the industry of $1,013 million last year, compared with a total net profit of $874 million, is already an equitable share of our business?
Mr Howe-What is the fee for this speech?
Mr BAUME-That is the sort of interjection that I would expect from the honourable member. It reflects the level of his thinking and the level, I believe, of his contribution to this House. The next point I make relates to the bureaucratic control of this industry which honourable members opposite seem so determined to pursue. Mr Morgan said:
There seems to be a belief in the community that a remote bureaucracy which makes decisions and rules about practical matters will have greater wisdom and virtue than those who have a vested interest in the economic activity it is regulating. I have found little evidence to support this belief. My experience is that the best decisions and the best management come only from tbose who have a position of real accountability in a direct personal way for performance.
In Australia any new minerals development project may well have to run the gauntlet of submissions to, and approvals from, up to 20 government instrumentalities, at the same time governments have stretched their constitutional competence to embrace matters which to the industry appear to be remote from the intention of earlier legislators.
It is'difficult for the community at large to comprehend the impact of the multiple interventions by government on resource projects, but there is no doubt that the result is increased cost, further delay and longer response times. None of these things can possibly benefit the man in the street, nor, in my opinion, can they do much to improve or protect his quality of life.
I believe that it is a shame that more members of the Labor Party did not go along to hear Mr Morgan's address. I hope that it had some sort of impact on the honourable member for Blaxland who attended the address. It seems a shame to me that so many members of the Labor Opposition are convinced that this mining honeypot is bottomless. The reality is that it is very risky business and I believe the Australian mining industry is doing a magnificent job, particularly under the present Government.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (lO.55)~I wish to raise in this House tonight a matter which is be-
ginning to cause deep concern in my electorate;
that is, a decision by the Department of Transport to locate a new international air terminal in the northern corner of Perth Airport. The matters involved are complex and I intend to raise them frequently in this place. At this preliminary stage, however, I want to place on record the grave exception taken by the Belmont Council, a local authority in my area, its members and officers, to the Department's decision and to the accompanying propaganda campaign being conducted among residents living near the airport. They are not complaining because they want the airport removed-let me make that quite clear. They are complaining because they do not believe they are being properly consulted and honestly dealt with and because they take the view that the propaganda campaign of the Department does not accurately portray the recommendations made by a joint State-Commonwealth committee on which the Council was represented.
That committee met for a number of years and contained representatives of a number of government departments, both State and Federal, and of a number of local authorities. The committee produced a series of recommendations and the Council believed that those recommendations would be acted upon. In the present process it believes that the environmental problems of its citizens have not been properly considered. It takes particular exception to an article which appears on page 2 of the Department's leaflet, which has been widely distributed in my electorate in association with the Department's efforts at explaining the proposed new terminal. That leaflet purports to represent the joint State-Commonwealth committee's recommendations. In it the Department implies that the committee firstly, recommended without qualification that Perth Airport be developed as the sole primary airport in the Perth region and, secondly, determined that a new north-south parallel runway was not justified on cost effective grounds and was not necessary on environmental grounds.
Both implications are misleading. In the first place, whilst the committee suggested that Perth Airport should be the sole primary airport for the Perth region until the year 2004, it further said that development should follow the production of a master plan based on a detailed analysis of airport development options and their impact on the environment and land use. On the Department's own admission, no such master plan has been

Appointments to Statutory Authorities under the Minister for Primary Industry
(Question No. 1011)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 1 Apri11981:
What are the (a) names, (b) dates and terms of appointment and (c) salaries of all persons appointed to the Boards and Commissions of Statutory Authorities under his jurisdiction.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

The following details on names, dates and terms of appointment, and salaries of public office holders ofstatutory authorities under my responsibility are those applicable at 30 April 1981. Details on deputies to Members are not included. However, when deputies attend meetings in place of Members, the daily sitting fee applies.
It should be noted that the salaries of all those listed, other than my departmental officers are set by the Remuneration Tribunal and that membership salaries of all listed authorities, other than those dealing with research, are industry funded. .
Statutory research authorities are funded either jointly by the Commonwealth and industry on a dollar-for-dollar basis or by the Commonwealth alone and members are eligible for a daily sitting fee from those funds.

Benoxaprofen: Marketing as Opren
(Question No. 1073)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) Is his Department evaluating the drug Benoxaprofen, marketed in the United Kingdom as Opren.
(2) If so, when are the results likely to (a) become known and (b) be published.
(3) If the results are successful, will the drug be placed on the National Health Scheme list.
Mr MacKeJlar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) (a) I am unable to state when the results will become known although it is likely that the evaluation reports will be considered by the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee before the end of this year.
(b) Results of the evaluation ofdrugs are not published, but are communicated in detail to the sponsoring drug companies as soon as practicable after their consideration by the ADEC.
(3) Drugs are considered for listing as pharmaceutical benefits under the National Health Scheme following a specific request from the company concerned or other interested parties. This consideration is given by the Pbarmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee, which is the independent statutory body established under the National Health Act to advise the Minister for Health on the listing of pharmaceutical benefits. The company has applied foriisting of Benoxaprofen and this will be considered if and when marketing approval is granted.

Drugs: Removal from Pharmaceutical Benefits
(Question No. 1176)
Mr Armitage asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
Further to his answer to my question No. 634 (Hansard, 2 April 1981, page 1329) relating to drugs taken off the pharmaceutical benefits list since 18 October 1980, which drugs on the list referred to in part (4) of the answer to question No. 634 are available to treat the conditions outlined in the reply to part (3) of the question.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
There is a wide range of drugs available as pharmaceutical benefits which are not restricted in regard to the conditions for which they may be prescribed as pharmaceutical benefits.
The selection of a suitable alternative pharmaceutical benefit for the treatment of a condition outlined in the reply to part (3) of question No. 634is primarily amalter for the individual patient's medical adviser to determine having due regard for the nature and severity of the condition, possible interactions with concomitant medication, patient idiosyncrasies and any other factors relevant to the individual patient.

Quarantine: Wood Eating Carpenter Ants
(Question No. 1695)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the New Zealand newspaper The Dominion of 6 February 1981 that wood eating carpenter ants were detected at Wellington in a container from Japan.
(2) If so, did the New Zealand Department of Agriculture and Fisheries advise his Department of the find, and supply details of the source and nature ofinfestation.
(3) Do Australian quarantine officials give details of similar finds at Australian ports to the New Zealand Department of Agriculture and Fisheries; if not, will he take active steps to develop such co-operation in quarantine matters between Australia and New Zealand.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. However, for years wood carpenter ants have been regularly intercepted at Australian ports of entry and appropriate quarantine action has been taken.
(2) No. Given quarantine surveillance arrangements which apply at Australian ports of entry, it is not considered necessary for New Zealand to advise Australia of routine pest interceptions made at New Zealand ports of entry.
(3) No. The specific arrangements proposed are not regarded as necessary. However, an arrangement does exist whereby New Zealand advises Australia and Australia advises New Zealand of any infestations detected in plants or goods moving across the Tasman.

(I) How many lepers are in Australia and what is their distribution by State and Territory.
(2) How many cases of leprosy have been reported in each State and Territory in each of the last 3 years.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In view of the stigma attached to the word 'leper' and the now curahle nature of the disease, it is desirable that sutTerers from leprosy be no longer referred to as lepers but as cases of leprosy. It has been the practice that all cases of leprosy, regardless of cure, remain registered for life. Annual notifications are small and the number of active cases at any one time is a mere fraction of those remaining on the register. The figures set out below are cumulative and do not take account of patients who may have recently died and have not been removed from the register since the last comprehensive review. Antarctica: Animal and Bird Diseases
(Question No. 1742)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What procedures have been adopted to ensure that animal and bird diseases are not introduced to the indigenous wildlife of Antarctica by (a) Australian expeditioners and (b) other nations with bases in the Antarctic.

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The 'Agreed Measures', developed and agreed to by the members of the Antarctic Treaty, detail provisions for the protection of all native Antarctic wildlife and for the prevention of the introduction of pests or disease into the Treaty area. Australia gave the 'Agreed Measures' force oflaw under the Antarctic Treaty (Environment Protection) Act which was proclaimed in June 1980. The Antarctic Division is administratively responsible for the Act including a permit system which is used to monitor and control activities which may have an adverse effect on the native fauna and flora or their habitat.
In accordance with Section 19 (I) of this Act, unless specifically authorised by permit, it is unlawful to take to the Antarctic any animal, plant, virus, bacterium, yeast or fungus that is not indigenous to the Antarctic. This provision does not apply to the introduction of animals and plants into Antarctica for use as food provided that they are used for this purpose and are kept under stringent conditions. Live poultry cannot be introduced.
The Division has actively implemented operational procedures consistent with the Agreed Measures. The growth in the use of containers and the unitisation of cargo and supplies has lessened the risk of contamination to the Antarctic environment. The cleanliness of containers is checked prior to their shipment from Australia. Unitised cargo loading has reduced the need for timber packaging. Other features of the Division's operations which promote environmental protectionare:
No live animals are introduced to Antarctica or subAntarctic Islands. Husky dogs are bred at Mawson station and there is infrequent exchange of husky dogs between Antarctic stations.
Annual provision of new clothing, footwear, cooking utensils and machinery reduces significantly the likelihood of germs surviving on inanimate objects.
With few exceptions, frozen meats, vegetables and poultry products taken to stations are produced in Australia, and hence are free from most of the major pests and diseases.
Immediately prior to departure to Antarctica, all heavy machinery (ie tractors, motorised tobaggans and sledges) is steamed clean and if appropriate painted.
Once identified any possible source of quarantine threat is communicated to Antarctic stations and resupply vessels for their information and appropriate action.
In the history of Australia's Antarctic operations, no infection has been detected by quarantine inspectors in Antarctic resupply vessels.
(b) Antarctic Treaty Consultative Parties are bound by the provisions and procedures of the Agreed Measures for the Conservation of Antarctic Fauna and Flora. However, it is considered that itinerant yachts and foreign fishing trawlers operating in the Southern Ocean constitute a quarantine risk and there is a need for greater surveillance in this area.
Antarctica: Garbage Disposal
(Question No. 1743)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(1) How is garbage and effluent disposed of at Australian Antarctic bases.
(2) Are the methods employed different from those adopted at the Antarctic bases of other nations; if so, in which way.

Answers to Questions

7 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2207

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The present situation is:
CaseyAll sewage and sullage is piped untreated to the sea. Burnable garbage is incinerated whilst remaining garbage
is disposed to landfill.
Davis and MawsonSolid human waste is incinerated. The products are ash
which is disposed to landfill. Liquid human waste and sullage is piped untreated away
from buildings whence it drains to the sea. Burnable garbage is incinerated whilst remaining garbage
is disposed to landfill.
In respect to future developments, current proposals as presented to the PWC provide that:
Liquid waste (human and sullage) will be biologically treated and effluent will be piped to the sea.
Sludge from the treatment process will be returned to Australia.
Burnable garbage will be incinerated. Other garbage .will be compacted and returned to Australia. Large metal objects will be cut up and returned to Australia.
(2) Twelve countries are operating stations in Antarctica. We have information concerning waste disposal practices of all these countries with the exception of Poland, Chile and Argentina.
The foHowing summarises disposal methods used for human waste and sullage.

Method

Number of stations where employed

Piping untreated waste to sea

4

Waterborne system using recirculated

effluent from which solids have been

removed and incinerated

1

Landfill . . . . . . . . . .

2

Chemical toilets with sullage piped to

sea or ice . . . . . . . . .

2

Untreated waste piped to ice disposal

I

Wastes tankered to landfill area

1

Information concerning garbage disposal is not as clear as for human waste and sullage. The method commonly used is one or a combination of the following:
Landfill. Incineration. Disposal to sea from ships. Disposal to sea by placing on pack ice. Returning to country of origin.
From the information giVe'll in answer to Question I, waste disposal methods used at Australian stations are approximately similar to those used, at the present time, by other countries.
In the future it is proposed that Australia should treat liquid waste and disposal of garbage by incineration and/or returning it to Australia. By comparison, no countries are known, at the present time, to be treating liquid waste as is proposed for Australian stations. Two countries at least are using chemical toilets.
If the proposals to treat liquid wastes are approved, Australia would be establishing a higher standard of waste disposal than is used at the present time by other countries.

This higher standard would minimise the impact of waste disposal upon the Antarctic environment.
Antarctica: Preservation of Historic Buildings
(Question No. 1744)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Who is responsible for care and maintenance of the two huts on Ross Island, Antarctica, used by Captain Scott.
(2) Has Australia made any contribution to the preservation of these historic buildings; if so, what.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) The huts on Ross Island are the responsibility of New Zealand. The NZ Huts Restoration Committee, which was formed in 1959-60, undertakes care and maintenance of the huts. The Committee is accommodated within the NZ Department of Science and Industrial Research and draws on the following organisations; Royal NZ Navy; Historical Places Trust; Ministry of Works; NZ Antarctic Society; Dominion Museum and the NZ Antarctic Division.
Hut caretakers each summer are unpaid volunteers and are kitted by the NZ Antarctic Division and are transported to Antarctica by US aircraft. Up to one thousand visitors inspect the huts each year.
(2) Australia has made no contribution to the preservation of the huts.
Antarctica: Origin of Meat and Vegetable Foodstuffs
(Question No. 1745)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What meat and vegetable foodstuffs at Australian Antarctic bases were obtained from non-Australian sources in each of the last 3 years.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
With few exceptions, meat and vegetable foodstuffs supplied to Australian Antarctic stations are Australian produce. It is estimated that less than one per cent of food supplied is of foreign origin.
Foreign meat and vegetable foods supplied over the last three years includes small quantities of smoked salmon, tinned and dried fish, dried field rations and some condiments. Some selected foods have also been supplied for the benefit of exchange scientists wintering at Australian Antarctic stations.
A detailed statement offood supplied to Australian Antarctic stations from non-Australian sources over the last three years can be compiled from existingrecords. However, this compilation would be time-consuming because of the need to consult procurement documents and shipping manifests. If the honourable member requests it, I will ask for this to be done.

Stage 2 of Central Services Complex, Mitchell, Australian Capital Territory
(Question No. 1835)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What are the reasons for the 23rd Annual Report of the National Capital Development Commission showing the completed cost of erection of Stage 2 of the Central Services Complex, Mitchell, Australian Capital Territory, as $3.217m, whilst the 1979-80 Annual Report of Department of Housing and Construction shows the completed cost as $3.16m.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows: '
I am advised by the National Capital Development Commission that:
The completed cost of$3.16m for the Stage 2 of the Central Services Complex at Mitchell as shown in the 1979-80 Annual Report of the Department of Housing and Construction refers to the building contract only.
The completed cost of $3.217m for the Stage 2 of the Central Services Complex as shown in the 23rd Annual Report of the National Capital Development Commission refers to the total project and includes, in addition to the building contract, separate contract works covering the installation of hydraulic platform pits and the installation of security equipment.
Aboriginal Housing
(Question No. 1843)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) What action has been taken by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs on the recommendation contained in the 5th Annual Report for 1979-80 of the Commissioner for Community Relations (page 58) that public housing appropriate to the needs, values and lifestyles of Aboriginal people in all States and the Northern Territory should be provided.
(2) In what way would acceptance of this recommendation require changes in the policy or practices of the Department.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) and (2) My Department's housing programs have for some years been directed to ensuring the provision of accommodation of a type and at locations suitable to the range of needs, values and lifestyles of Aboriginal people. The programs comprise grants to State and Northern Territory housing authoriti~, grants-in-aid to Aboriginal housing associations, loans by the Aboriginal Development Commission for the purchase or erection of housing, and the provision of hostel accommodation through Aboriginal Hostels Limited. The grants to State and Northern Territory housing authorities are intended to cater for Aboriginals who do not meet the

eligibility criteria under the welfare housing programs administered by the States and the Northern Territory or who, because of their position on the waiting list or because of the unavailability of suitable accommodation at the required location, would not be provided with housing under those programs within a reasonable time.

Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1957)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(1) Has the South Eastern Fisheries Committee been required to undertake a study of the tuna and trawl fisheries and to report to the Australian Fisheries Council in September 1981.
(2) Was the South Eastern Fisheries Committee the body which originally recommended to the Australian Fisheries Council that the freeze on the southern bluefin tuna fishery be lifted.
(3) Can the study now asked of the South Eastern Fisheries Committee be done in a fair and unbiased manner, given the on-the-record position of the Committee concerning the lifting of the freeze.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (3) Yes.
Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1958)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 198 I:
(1) Did he say in a recent statement that fishermen planning to enter the southern bluefin tuna fishery should not base their expectations of profitability on taking southern bluefin tuna.
(2) If so, for what other reason would fishermen planning to enter the fishery seek a licence, unless they wanted to take southern bluefin tuna.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) an (2) I said that fishermen planning to enter the fishery should not base their expectations of profitability on taking southern bluefin tuna because it is possible that new entrants to the fishery could make substantial investments on the, assumption that they will have long term access to the fishery. The question of long term access will depend on what future management arrangements, if any, apply in the trawl and tuna fisheries in south eastern Australia. I felt it was responsible to warn persons considering making an investment to fish for southern bluefin tuna, that management measures might eventuate which could affect the viability of such investment.
Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1959)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the inquiry being conducted by the Senate Standing Committee on Trade and Resources into the responsibilities of the Commonwealth Government for the development of thc Australian fishing industry, and that that Committee, in hearings held in recent weeks, has heard evidence from his -Department, the Australian Fishing Industry Council and other interested bodies.
(2) If so, why has he chosen to take a major management decision in terms of the southern bluefin tuna fishery, without

awaiting the outcome and recommendations of that Committee given the very detailed terms of reference of the Committee and that hearings of the Committee now are being held.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) No-one could seriously suggest that management decisions of any significance should be postponed pending the outcome of a Senate inquiry, particularly in cases, such as this, where the issue is not referred to in the terms of reference of the inquiry.
Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1960)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(I) Did he recently express his concern that Australians could be prevented from fishing for souther bluefin tuna when there is an agreement which enables Japanese fishermen to do so.
(2) Following the 1980 Australia-Japan fisheries renegotiation talks, did he set up a working group to inquire into the potential for Australian fishermen to participate in longline fishing for tuna which currently accounts for 30 per cent of the souther bluefin tuna catch in the Australian fishing zone.
(3) If so, how can a major management decision be taken concerning this fishery, prior to receiving the report andrecommendations from the working group into tuna long-lining which he has set up.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Such a working group was established.
(3) Whether or not there is a freeze on entry by Australians to the southern bluefin tuna pole and line fishery is not critical to the task of the working group.

(Question No. 15)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Industrial Relations, upon notice, on 20 August 1980:
(1) Has he caused any inquiry to be made in view of recent l'eports on the ABC television program Four Corners about problems being experienced by workers in Whyalla, South Australia which had led to general poor relations between Broken Hill Pty Ltd and its workforce or has any section of his Department, on its own initiative, made such an inquiry.

Superannuation: Adriatic Life Insurance
(Question No. 452)
Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) Is it a fact, that Adriatic Life Insurance, with cooperation from the Department of Defence, is promoting a section 79 superannuation fund for members of the RAAF with contributions up to $1,000 per annum accepted over the sum being contributed to the Defence Force Retirement and Death Benefits Fund.
(2) If so, was the Tax Commissioner approached to ignore the service pension when calculating allowable contributions to this fund.
(3) What rate of interest is being used to calculate allowable contributions to the fund.
(4) Is an increase in the maximum sum allowed at retirement pending or has a special case for Defence personnel been negotiated.
(5) If a special case was negotiated, will this be advised to all insurance companies and would it also apply to the public service as a whole.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l), (2) and (3) Because of express provisions in the income tax law prohibiting him from diVUlging information respecting the affairs of any other person, the Commissioner of Taxation is unable to comment on the income tax affairs of Adriatic Life Insurance or individual members oftheRAAF.
As a general proposition, however, a member of the Defence Forces or, for that matter, any Commonwealth or other

employee, may apply to join a public superannuation fund established and maintained in accordance with section 79 of the Income Tax Assessment Act, provided that his contributions to, and benefits available from, all classes of superannuation funds do not exceed the maximum amounts laid down by the Commissioner.
(4) The Commissioner has recently increased the maximum contributions which may be paid to and the benefits which may be made available from all classes of superannuation funds.
(5) The new levels of contribution rates and maximum benefits have been made public and all insurance companies would be well aware of the increased benefits.

Arcata Investments Pty Ltd
(Question No. 454)
Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(l) With reference to my question No. 453 of 24 February 1981 to the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, concerning the company which was given an immediate profit of $300,000 by the Receiver of Cambridge Credit Corporation, namely Arcata Investments Pty Limited, will he investigate whether this transaction led to exploitation of Australian taxpayers as well as the creditors of Cambridge Credit Corporation through a scheme of tax evasion using overseas tax shelters.
(2) Is Arcata Investments Pty Limited a Nauru-based company with the ultimate parent company being a Hong Kong company.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) The matters raised by the honourable Member have been referred to the Commissioner of Taxation who is responsible for the administration of the income tax law. The Commissioner has advised that the secrecy provisions of that law preclude him from commenting in any way on the taxation affairs of any person or company or on any action that might be taken in respect of any person or company.

Television Translator at Warburton, Victoria
(Qeuestion No. 964)
Mr Cunningham asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) Did his predecessor give a commitment to install a television translator in the Upper Yarra area north-east of Melbourne at Warburton.
(2) If so, when will work commence on installation of the translator.
(3) Will the translator's installation mean that all residents in that area will be able to receive adequate television reception.
(41Will there be a cost involved for residents in the area
resulting from the installation of the translator.
Mr SincJair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The proposal calls for establishment of facilities to provide the township with programs from the three Melbourne commercial stations and the ABC. Suitable provision is being made for the SBS to participate if this should be considered desirable.
At this stage the three Melbourne commercial television stations have been offered the grant of licences to serve the locality and my Department is at present co-ordinating final technical operating conditions for the translators with the stations.
The national service translator is scheduled for completion in the 1981-82 financial year; subject of course to the level of funding made available by the Government.
(3) These translators are designed to serve the town of Warburton in the Upper Yarra Shire of Victoria. As with most television installations of this nature there may he some isolated pockets that will not receive adequate coverage. However, my Department is unable to precisely determine the number and extent of these or the most appropriate method of improving coverage in them until the translators are operating.
(4) Those residents choosing to watch programs from the translators will have to employ equipment capable of receiving UHF transmissions. As stated in my reply to Mr Milton,

(Question No. 257) the modification of television receivers without UHF capability requires the installation of UHF to VHF down converters. A commercially available 'UHF settop converter' retails at approximately $80.00. Good reception of UHF transmissions also requires the installation of a UHF antenna. A cost of $80.00-$90.00 could be expected to be incurred for the complete installation of a new antenna of this type. This figure could be reduced if the existing VHF antenna hardware is utilised. Costs for the UHF antenna only range from approximately $25.00-$35.00. A diplexer unit which allows the use of VHF and UHF antennae with a common feedline retails at approximately $15.00.

Engagement by Officers in Outside Employment: Department of Communications
(Question No. 1391)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Have any officers and employees of the Department of Communications, been granted permission to engage in outside employment, if so, how many.
(2) What is the division and approximate salary level of each officer or employee granted permission, and what was the nature of outside employment approved in each case,
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Currently within this Department only one formal approval to engage in outside employment exists.
(2) The current approval is in respect of a Fourth Division officer with the annual salary of $16,300 per annum approximately. The outside employment involves work on a Communication Project for the Australian Army which has been contracted to a private company. The officer concerned is on leave without pay for the period of outside employment.

Answers to Questions

Recognition of Aborigines, Torres Strait Islanders and South Sea Islanders

(Question No. 1847)

Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the

Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice on 9

Apri11981:

'

.( ~) What ~ction has been taken by the Department of Abongmal Affairs on the recommendation of the Commissioner for Community Relations in his. 5th Annual Report for I ??9-S0 that G.o~ernment agencies should grant equal recogmtlOn to Aborlgmes, Torres Strait Islanders and South Sea Islanders.

(2) Doe~ t~e experience of Departmental officers support the Commissioner's assessment that distinctions made between the 3 groups in delivery of services and assessment of needs has created tensions and disharmony.

Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(1) An Inter-departmental Committee established in 1975 to examine the situation of South Sea Islanders in Australia recommended that although there was evidence of some disadvantage, extension of the special assistance available to Aboriginals and Torres Strait Islanders was not warranted.

The Committee found that South Sea Islanders were not fully aware of existing general community assistance programs and benefits. As a result, when the then Minister for Social Security tabled a summary of the Committee's main findings on 13 S~ptember 1978 she announced that the needs of ~outh Sea ~slanders would be given specific attention by a SOCial worker m her Department working in conjunction with other staff in regional offices.

(2) Officers of my Department have informed me that although distinctions in assistance programs available to South Sea Islanders, and Aboriginals and Torres Strait Islanders have been blamed for exacerbating tensions between the groups, the Department's experience has been that the causes of such tension, where it exists, are more deeply rooted.

5th Annual Report of the Commissioner for Community Relations
(Question No. 1848)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Does the Minister support the following statement of the Commissioner for Community Relations at page 99 of his 5th Annual Report for 1979-80 that the solution to the white problems lies with the white governments and white bureaucracies; the answer to black problems lies with the powers that oppressed the Aborigines in the first place.
(2) Ifnot, why not.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) and (2) The quoted statement comes at the end of a section of the Commissioner's Annual Report in which he discusses complaints he received about 'Special Programs for Disadvantaged Groups' implemented by Commonwealth and State Governments. The Commissioner indicates his full suppor.t for Governments in implementing special measures and mdlcates that these do not constitute racial discrimination for

the purposes of the International Convention on the Elimination ofAll Forms ofRacial Discrimination or the Racial Discrimination Act, but are required to ensure adequate development and protection of human rights for disadvantaged racial or ethnic groups. It remains the policy of the Commonwealth Government to maintain and develop special programs for the advancement of the Aboriginal people.
Australian Fishing Zone
(Question No. 1863)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
What action has been taken to enforce the 200 mile fishing zone in relation to Norfolk Island?
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Department of Primary Industry monitors reports of foreign fishing vessel activity in the Australian Fishing Zone (AFZ), including the AFZ around Norfolk Island and has arranged for the charter of aircraft on a number of occasions to investigate alleged infringements when timely information regarding such incidents has been received. In addition, the Australian Government has informed the relevant foreign governments of incidents involving foreign flag fishing vessels suspected of illegal activities in the -AFZ around Norfolk Island.
Licences have been issued which permit a number of foreign fishing vessels to fish in the AFZ off Norfolk Island under prescribed terms and conditions. The operations of such vessels are monitored by means of a mandatory radio reporting systemwhich requires each vessel to provide details of its movements and activities (including catches) to the Department of Primary Industry (via the Australian Coastal Surveillance Centre) on a regular basis.
Surveillance and enforcement measures in the AFZ are under continuous review, including those relating to areas of the AFZ surrounding external territories, and will be further considered during the forthcoming review of the existing Australian civil coastal surveillance system, the report "n which is due to be presented to the Australian Government in mid-1981.
Southern Bluefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1956)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(1) Did he say in a recent statement concerning the lifting of the freeze on the southern bluefin tuna fishery, that new entrants,.if any, will not be able to eStablish access rights under any future management regime on the same basis as fishermen who have operated in the fishery to date.
(2) If so, does his reference to a future management regime indicate that there will be a future management regiine to replace the freeze on entry.
(3) Does the statement referred to in part (I) indicate that he intends establishing some form of second-class licence in the southern bluefin tuna fishery, in that certain licences will not carry with them the same rights as licences which existed prior to the lifting of the freeze.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) No.

Answers to Questions

(3) No. The statement indicates that if in future it proved necessary to control or even reduce the number of boats in the fishery, those who had been in the fishery for a period of time would be given priority over new entrants to the fishery.
Southern B1uefin Tuna Fishery
(Question No. 1961)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(1) Did he recently quote an economic study, or survey, undertaken by his Department as at least a substantial part of the basis on which he took (he decision (0 lift the freeze on entry to the southern bluefin tuna fishery.
(2) If so, has the Australian Fishing Industry Council informed him that they regard the economic survey carried out by his Department as being inadequate, in that it contains old information which, in the view of the Council, is of very limited benefit, and also that it contains important gaps that throw the value of the entire document into doubt.

(3) Did the Australian Fishing Industry Council also request him to update and upgrade the economic survey which he used as the basis for his decision before any substantive decision is taken on the longlerm future of the southern bluefin tuna fishery.
(4) If so, why did he choose to ignore the advice of the Council concerning the value or the economic survey.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No. The lifting of the freeze was based on the decision of the Australian Fisheries Council in 1979, confirmed in 1980 and supported by substantial sectors of the Australian fishing industry.
(2) See (1).
(3) Yes, although it is difficult to see how 1979-80 information could be regarded as seriously out of date.
(4) I did not ignore the advice of the Australian Fishing Industry Council.
